When One Slave Strikes First in a Slave-Slave Conflict
Ruling on rights in slave-slave conflict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for determining which master has the stronger claim when one slave initiates a strike, and apply the Hanafi principles of substitution and equivalence to subsequent scenarios including death.

Core Idea
The master of the later striker has the stronger claim because his slave was sound when injured.

When two slaves fight and one strikes first, the legal rights of their masters depend critically on the sequence of events. The Hanafi school uses principles of substitution and equivalence to determine restitution, placing the later striker's master in a stronger position because his slave was sound before the injury. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq illustrates these rules through several detailed scenarios, though the text is incomplete at the end.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initiating event
Determine which slave struck first. This is the key fact that sets the rights of the masters.
The first striker is called al-badi'; the later striker is al-lahiq.
2
Compare the rights of the masters based on the condition of their slaves
The master of the later striker has a right over a sound slave (the initiator), while the master of the initiator has a right only over a wounded slave. Therefore, the later striker's master has a stronger claim.
The passage explains why the later striker's master can demand a sound slave in return, while the initiator's master cannot demand a sound slave.
3
Analyze the consequences of payment or ransom
If the later striker's master chooses ransom, the initiator's slave is released from liability. If the later striker kills the initiator, the initiator's value becomes a debt on the later striker's slave, with options for ransom or surrender.
The principle of substitution: a deceased slave is replaced by his value, and rights transfer accordingly.
4
Trace the flow of rights when a slave dies
If the later striker's slave dies, the initiator's master may lose his right to compensation because the right was over the slave's person, which is now extinct. However, the passage is cut off, and the full resolution requires the missing text.
The incomplete ending may affect the final rule; refer to complete sources for the full ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن سبق أحدهما بالضربة خير مولى مولى البادئ لأن البداية من مولى اللاحق لا تفيد لأن حق اللاحق في عبد صحيح كامل الرقبة، فإذا دفع إلى البادئ عبداً مشجوجاً كان للأحق أن يسترد ثانياً لأنه يقول عبدك شج عبدي وهو صحيح ودفعت إلى عبدك بدل تلك الشجة فيكون لي والبداية من مولى البادئ بالدفع مفيدة لأن حق البادئ ثبت في عبد مشجوج فمتى دفعه مشجوجاً لا يكون له أن يسترده فكان دفعه مفيداً، فإن دفعه فالعبد للمدفوع إليه ولا شيء للدافع لأنه لو رجع البادئ بشيء كان للمدفوع إليه أن يرجع عليه ثانياً لأن حقه في رقبة عبد فلا يفيد رجوع البادئ، وإن فداه خير مولى اللاحق بين الدفع والفداء لأنه ظهر عند صحیح البادئ عن الجناية بالفداء وصار كأنه لم يجن. وإن جنى عليه العبد اللاحق فإن مات البادئ كانت قيمته في عنق الثاني يدفع بها أو الفداء، فإن فداه بقيمة الميت رجع في تلك القيمة بأرش جراحته عبداً لأن بالفداء أظهر عبد اللاحق عن الجناية وصار كأنه لم يجن وإنما جنى عليه البادئ، والبادئ وإن مات فالقيمة قامت مقامه لأنه حق قائم مقامه. وإن دفعه رجع بأرش شجة عبده في عنقه ويخير المدفوع إليه بين الدفع والفداء لأن المدفوع قام مقام الميت الشاج، وإن مات العبد القاتل خير مولى العبد البادئ، وإن فداه أو دفع بطل حقه شج اللاحق البادئ كان اللاحق مشجوجاً فثبت حق مولى البادئ في عبد مشجوج فثبت فيما وراء الشجة فمات لا إلى خلف لما مات العبد القاتل فبطل حق مولى البادئ في شجة عبده. ولو مات البادئ من شيء آخر سوى الجناية وبقي اللاحق خير مولى البادئ ويقال له إن شئت فاعف عن مولى اللاحق ولا سبيل لواحد منهما على الآخر، وإن شئت ادفع أرش شجة اللاحق وطالبه بحقك، وإن دفع إلى صاحبه أرش عبده يرجع بأرش جناية عبده فيدفع مولى اللاحق عبده بها أو يفديه. أما المفهوم فلأن مولى البادئ بجنايته إذا دفع كان لمولى اللاحق أن يطالبه بأرش شجة عبده لأنه حين
And if one of them strikes first, the master of the second (the later striker) is given a choice over the master of the initiator, because the initiative from the master of the later one is not beneficial, since the right of the later one is over a sound slave, complete. So if he gives to the initiator a wounded slave, the one with the right (the later) has the right to take it back a second time, because he says: 'Your slave injured my slave while he was sound, and I gave you a substitute for that injury, so it is mine.' But the initiative from the master of the initiator in giving is beneficial, because the right of the initiator is established over a wounded slave; so when he gives him wounded, he does not have the right to take it back, so his giving is beneficial. If he gives, then the slave belongs to the recipient, and nothing is due to the giver, because if the initiator sought recourse for something, the recipient would have recourse against him a second time, since his right is over the entire slave, so the initiator's recourse is not beneficial. And if he ransoms, the master of the later one is given a choice between surrender and ransom, because the initiator's slave is released from liability through ransom, making it as if he did not commit the crime. And if the later slave (the one who struck second) injures the initiator, and the initiator dies, then his value is a debt upon the second slave, to be paid by surrender or ransom. If he ransoms by paying the value of the deceased, he recovers in that value the arsh of his own slave's injury, because through ransom the later slave is freed from liability, making it as if he did not commit the crime, and only the initiator committed it. And the initiator, even if dead, is replaced by his value; that is a right that stands in his place. And if he surrenders, he recovers the arsh of his slave's wound from the surrendered slave's neck, and the recipient of the surrendered slave has a choice between surrender and ransom, because the surrendered slave stands in the place of the deceased initiator. And if the killing slave dies, the master of the initiator slave is given a choice. If he ransoms or surrenders, the right of the later one's injury to the initiator becomes void. [The text ends.]
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where one slave strikes first. The rights differ: the late-striker's master has a stronger claim because his slave was sound when injured. The initiator's master has a right only over a wounded slave. Various sub-scenarios follow: if the later slave kills the initiator, the initiator's value becomes a debt; if the later slave dies, the initiator's master has a choice. The passage is cut off at the end, but it outlines a detailed set of rights and options for the masters, highlighting how the sequence of injury affects the strength of claims. The Hanafi system uses principles of substitution and equivalence: a deceased slave is replaced by his value, and the right transfers accordingly.

Key Terms
بادئ (badi'): The slave who initiated the strike; the first aggressor.
لاحق (lahiq): The slave who responded later; the second striker.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a wound or injury short of death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on a slave-slave conflict where one strikes first, which of the following is true?
A) The master of the first striker has the stronger claim because he acted first.
B) The master of the second striker has the stronger claim because his slave was sound when injured.
C) Both masters have equal claims because both slaves caused injury.
D) The claim is determined by the value of the slaves, not the sequence.

Key Takeaway
In slave-slave conflicts, the master of the slave who was sound when struck has the stronger claim, regardless of who struck first.

Mutual Injury by Two Slaves and Masters' Options
Detailed ruling on mutual injury by two slaves and options of their masters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling for mutual injury by two slaves to determine the masters' options in various scenarios of striking order and death.

Core Idea
When two slaves mutually injure each other, each master may either hand over his slave or pay full compensation.

This lesson unpacks a classical Hanafi ruling on a scenario where two slaves fight and wound each other. The masters are given clear options to ensure full compensation for their own slave's injury, whether by exchanging the slaves or paying the monetary difference. Understanding these rules reveals the precision of Hanafi fiqh in treating slaves as property while protecting the owner's right to sound value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Basic Scenario
Both slaves strike each other simultaneously, and both recover from their injuries. The passage sets this as the default case (أصلا).
Simultaneous means no clear precedence.
2
State the General Rule
Each master is given a choice (خيار): either hand over his slave to the other master (دفع) or pay the full blood-money (أرش) for the injury his slave caused (فداء). They cannot later reclaim what they gave because each master's right is established in a complete slave.
The option is wajib (binding); they must choose one.
3
Analyze the Qiyas Basis
The rule is derived via qiyas from free persons who injure each other (qisas/diya). For slaves, the compensation is based on the diminution of market value (arsh). The 'illa is the master's property right, which is analogous to a free person's right to bodily integrity, but translated into value.
This is qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).
4
Examine the Variation When One Strikes First (al-Badi')
If one slave strikes first, the master of the later striker (al-lahiq) has a more favorable position because his right attached to a sound slave. The master of the first striker (al-badi') must accept a wounded slave or receive compensation, but cannot demand a sound one.
The text details why the later master's giving is beneficial and the first master's is not.
5
Examine Death Scenarios
If the first striker dies from the injury, his value becomes a debt on the second slave. If the killer slave dies, the first master's right in the wound is voided. The passage also covers death from other causes.
These sub-rules ensure each master receives the full value of a sound slave or the injury equivalent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبدان التقيا ومع كل واحد عصا فأضربا وبرئا خير مولى كل واحد منهما بالآخر ولا يتراجعان بشيء سوى ذلك لأن كل واحد منهما ملك عبده من صاحبه ولا يفيد التراجع لأنه لو رجع أحدهما لرجع الآخر لأن حق كل واحد منهما ثبت في رقبة كاملة فما يأخذ أحدهما من صاحبه فذاك بدل الآخر وتعلق به حقه فلا يفيد الرجوع، وإن اختار الفداء فدى كل واحد بجميع أرش جنايته لأنهما لما ضربا معاً فقد جنى كل واحد منهما على عبد صحيح فتعلق حق كل واحد من الموليين بعبد صحيح فيجب بدل عبد صحيح، وإن سبق أحدهما بالضربة خير المولى مولى البادئ لأن البداية من مولى اللاحق لا تفيد لأن حق اللاحق في عبد صحيح كامل الرقبة، فإذا دفع إلى البادئ عبداً مشجوجاً كان للأحق أن يسترد ثانياً لأنه يقول عبدك شج عبدي وهو صحيح ودفعت إلى عبدك بدل تلك الشجة فيكون لي والبداية من مولى البادئ بالدفع مفيدة لأن حق البادئ ثبت في عبد مشجوج فمتى دفعه مشجوجاً لا يكون له أن يسترده فكان دفعه مفيداً، فإن دفعه فالعبد للمدفوع إليه ولا شيء للدافع لأنه لو رجع البادئ بشيء كان للمدفوع إليه أن يرجع عليه ثانياً لأن حقه في رقبة عبد فلا يفيد رجوع البادئ، وإن فداه خير مولى اللاحق بين الدفع والفداء لأنه ظهر عند صحیح البادئ عن الجناية بالفداء وصار كأنه لم يجن. وإن جنى عليه العبد اللاحق فإن مات البادئ كانت قيمته في عنق الثاني يدفع بها أو الفداء، فإن فداه بقيمة الميت رجع في تلك القيمة بأرش جراحته عبداً لأن بالفداء أظهر عبد اللاحق عن الجناية وصار كأنه لم يجن وإنما جنى عليه البادئ، والبادئ وإن مات فالقيمة قامت مقامه لأنه حق قائم مقامه. وإن دفعه رجع بأرش شجة عبده في عنقه ويخير المدفوع إليه بين الدفع والفداء لأن المدفوع قام مقام الميت الشاج، وإن مات العبد القاتل خير مولى العبد البادئ، وإن فداه أو دفع بطل حقه شج اللاحق البادئ كان اللاحق مشجوجاً فثبت حق مولى البادئ في عبد مشجوج فثبت فيما وراء الشجة فمات لا إلى خلف لما مات العبد القاتل فبطل حق مولى البادئ في شجة عبده. ولو مات البادئ من شيء آخر سوى الجناية وبقي اللاحق خير مولى البادئ ويقال له إن شئت فاعف عن مولى اللاحق ولا سبيل لواحد منهما على الآخر، وإن شئت ادفع أرش شجة اللاحق وطالبه بحقك، وإن دفع إلى صاحبه أرش عبده يرجع بأرش جناية عبده فيدفع مولى اللاحق عبده بها أو يفديه. أما المفهوم فلأن مولى البادئ بجنايته إذا دفع كان لمولى اللاحق أن يطالبه بأرش شجة في شجة عبده لأنه حين وكان لمولى البادئ أن يدفع إليه العبد المدفوع ثانياً إليه عن حقه فلا يفيده الدفع وإنما دفع أرش شجة اللاحق لأنه متى دفع أرش عبد اللاحق فقد طهر البادئ عن الجناية وصار كأنه لم يجن وإنما جنى عليه العبد اللاحق فيخاطب مولى اللاحق بالدفع والفداء وأي ذلك اختار لا يبقى لواحد منهما على صاحبه سبيل لأنه وصل إلى كل واحد منهما حقه. وإن أبي مولى البادئ أن يدفع الأرش فلا شيء له في عتق الآخر فإن مولى البادئ كان مخيراً بين العفو وبين دفع الأرش والمطالبة شجة لعبده، فإذا امتنع من دفع الأرش صار مختاراً للعفو وصار كأنه قال عفوتك عن حقي فيبطل حقه. ولو مات اللاحق وبقي البادئ خير ،مولاه، فإن دفعه بطل حقه، وإن فداه بأرش عبده في الفداء لأن البادئ طاهر عن الجناية لعفو وأحدهما عن جناية نصف العبد ولا يزداد حقه فكذا هذا .
Two slaves met, each with a stick, and they struck each other and recovered. The master of each is given choice regarding the other, and they do not take back anything other than that, because each one has owned the slave of the other, and returning does not benefit, because if one returns the other would also return, since each one's right is established in a complete slave. So what one takes from the other is the substitute for the other, and his right attaches to it; thus returning does not benefit. If he chooses ransom, each one pays with the full blood-money of his injury, because when they struck together, each one inflicted injury on a sound slave, so the right of each master attaches to a sound slave, so the substitute of a sound slave is due. If one preceded in striking, the master of the one who began is given choice, because initiating from the master of the later one does not benefit, since the right of the later one is in a complete and sound slave. So if he gives to the initiator a slave with a wound, the one entitled can claim a second time, because he says, 'your slave wounded my slave while he was sound, and I gave to your slave the substitute of that wound, so it is mine.' And initiating from the master of the initiator by giving is beneficial, because the right of the initiator was established in a wounded slave, so when he gives him wounded, he cannot reclaim him; thus his giving is beneficial. If he gives him, the slave belongs to the recipient and nothing is due to the giver, because if the initiator recovers something, the recipient would reclaim from him a second time, since his right is in the slave's value. If he ransoms, the master of the later one is given choice between giving and ransom, because the initiator has become clear of the injury by ransom, as if he did not injure. If the later slave injures him and the initiator dies, his value is in the neck of the second, he gives it or ransoms. If he ransoms with the value of the dead slave, he recovers from that value the blood-money of his injury to a slave, because by ransom he clears the later slave from injury, as if he did not injure, only the initiator injured him. And if the initiator died, the value stands in his place because it is a right that stands in his place. If he gives him, he recovers the blood-money of his slave's wound from his neck, and the recipient is given choice between giving and ransom, because the given slave stands in place of the dead one who inflicted the wound. If the killer slave dies, the master of the initiating slave is given choice; if he ransoms or gives, his right becomes void...
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This is an expansion probe (sharh) on a detailed ruling from the matn regarding two slaves who mutually injure each other with sticks. The passage presents multiple scenarios and the legal options for their masters. The core principle is that each master's right is attached to the value of his slave. When both strike simultaneously, each master may either hand over his slave to the other (muqāṣṣa) or pay the full blood-money for the injury his slave caused. When one strikes first (al-bādī), the master of the first striker cannot demand a sound slave because his right was established on a wounded slave; thus the later master may either accept the wounded slave or demand compensation. If the first slave dies from the injury, his value becomes a debt upon the second slave. The passage also covers cases where one slave dies from other causes, and how the options change. The object is to ensure each master receives full compensation for loss of a sound slave, while preventing unjust enrichment. The ruling is based on qiyās and istiḥsān, as the Hanafi school uses analogical reasoning from cases of property damage.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injury to a slave, calculated as the diminution in value.
بادئ (bādi’): The one who strikes first in the mutual fight.
فداء (fidā’): Ransom payment to free the slave from liability or to avoid handing him over.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two slaves strike each other simultaneously, what are the options for their masters?
A) Only hand over their slaves to each other
B) Only pay blood-money for the injury
C) Either hand over the slave or pay the full arsh of the injury
D) Forgive without compensation

Key Takeaway
The master's right to a sound slave drives the option of either handing over the wounded slave or receiving full compensation, with nuanced rules for order of striking and death.

Liability for Death Caused by Animals in a Caravan
Ruling on liability when a camel in a caravan kills a person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules on liability for accidental killing by animals in a caravan and distinguish between liability of a direct agent (mubāshir) and a cause (mutasabbib).

Core Idea
In a caravan, liability for accidental killing falls on the 'āqilah of the person in control, not the animal owner.

The Hanafi school has detailed rules for liability when animals cause harm in caravans. This lesson examines a passage from al-Baḥr al-Rāʾiq that clarifies who is responsible — the leader, the driver, the rider, or the one who tied the animal. Understanding these distinctions is essential for applying the concept of causation versus direct action in Islamic tort law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios
List each scenario described: (1) saddle falls, (2) leader's camel tramples, (3) driver and leader together, (4) rider's camel tramples, (5) tied camel kills.
Each scenario has a distinct rule.
2
Determine the underlying principle
Note that liability attaches to the person who could have prevented the harm, i.e., the one in control (leader/driver) or the direct agent (rider). The animal owner is not automatically liable.
Negligence and causation are key.
3
Analyze the liability of multiple parties
When both leader and driver are present, they share liability equally. When a rider is directly causing, he alone bears the guarantee.
This shows how the rule distinguishes between control and direct action.
4
Examine the recovery mechanism
In the tied-camel case, the leader's 'āqilah pays first, then recovers from the tier's 'āqilah, because the tier is the ultimate cause.
This illustrates secondary liability and recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ولو) ساق دابة فوقع السرج على رجل فقتله ضمن) يعني إذا ساق دابة ولها سرج فوقع السرج على رجل فقتله ضمن عاقلته الدية وقد قدمناها بفروعها. قال رحمه الله : (وإن قاد قطاراً فوطئ بعير إنساناً ضمن عاقلة القائد الدية لأن القائد عليه حفظ القطار كالسائق وقد أمكنه التحرز عنه فصار متعدياً بالتقصير فيه والتسبب بلفظ التعدي أن ضمان النفس على العاقلة وضمان المال عليه في ماله. رجل له مزرعة فأكلها جمل غير فأخذه وحبسه في الأصطبل ثم وجد الجمل مكسور الرجل كيف الحكم بينهما في ذلك ؟ فقال : إن لم يكسر رجله في حبسه قالوا لا ضمان عليه وقد قالوا الضمان عليه ما لم يسلمه إلى صاحبه والرأي فيه إلى القاضي قال رحمه الله : (وإن كان معه سائق فعليهما) أي إذا كان مع القائد سائق تجب على عاقلتهما الضمان لاستوائهما في التسبب لأن قائد الواحد قائد الكل، وكذا سائقه لاتصال اللازمة، وأما البعير الذي هو راكبه فهو ضامن لما أصابه فيجب عليه وعلى القائد غير ما أصابه بالإيطاء فإن ذلك ضمانه على الراكب وحده لأنه جعل فيه مباشراً حتى جرت عليه أحكام المباشرة على ما بيناه. قال رحمه الله : (وإن ربط بعيراً على قطار رجع على عاقلة القائد بدية ما تلف به على عاقلة الرابط) أي إذا ربط رجل بعيراً على قطار والقائد لذلك القطار لا يعلم فوطئ البعير المربوط إنساناً فقتله فعلى عاقلة القائد ديته لأنه يمكنه أن يصون قطاره عن ربط غيره به فإذا ترك صيانته صار متعدياً بالتقصير وهو متسبب وفيه الدية على العاقلة كما في قتل الخطأ يرجعون بها على عاقلة الرابط لأنه هو الذي أوقعهم فيه، وإنما لا يجب الضمان على القائد ثم والرابط ابتداء مع أن كل واحد منهما متسبب لأن القود بمنزلة المباشرة بالنسبة إلى الربط لاتصال التلف به دون الربط فيجب فيه الضمان وحده ثم يرجع به عليه. قالوا : هذا إذا
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If he drove an animal and the saddle fell on a man and killed him, he guarantees' meaning if he drove an animal that has a saddle and the saddle fell on a man and killed him, his 'āqilah owes the diyah, and we have already presented it with its subbranches. He said: 'If he led a caravan and a camel trampled a man, the 'āqilah of the leader owes the diyah' because the leader is responsible for guarding the caravan like the driver, and he could have prevented it, so he became a transgressor by negligence, and causing by [his] negligence; the guarantee for life is on the 'āqilah, and for property on his own wealth. A man has a field, and a camel belonging to someone else ate it; he took it and imprisoned it in the stable, then found the camel with a broken leg. How is the judgment between them? He said: If he did not break its leg during imprisonment, they said there is no guarantee on him, and they said guarantee is on him until he returns it to its owner, and the opinion in that is for the judge. He said: 'If there was a driver with him, then on both' meaning if there is a driver with the leader, the guarantee is obligatory on their two 'āqilahs because they are equal in causing, because the leader of one is the leader of all, likewise its driver due to the connectedness of the string. As for the camel that is ridden, its rider is guarantor for what it hits, and he is obligated along with the leader for what it hits by trampling; that guarantee is on the rider alone because he is considered direct agent, so the rulings of direct agency apply to him as we explained. He said: 'If one tied a camel to a caravan, he recovers from the 'āqilah of the leader the diyah of what is lost, to be on the 'āqilah of the one who tied' meaning if a man tied a camel to a caravan while the leader of that caravan does not know, and the tied camel trampled a man and killed him, then the diyah is on the 'āqilah of the leader because he could have protected his caravan from others tying to it; when he left that protection, he became a transgressor by negligence, and he is a cause; the diyah is on the 'āqilah as in accidental killing, and they recover it from the 'āqilah of the one who tied because he caused them to be in that situation. The guarantee is not obligatory on the leader and the one who tied initially, although each is a cause, because the leader is like a direct agent in relation to the tying due to the connection of loss to it without the tying; so the guarantee is on him alone, then he recovers from the one who tied. They said: This is if...
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This is an expansion probe (sharh) on the rules of liability (ḍamān) for damages caused by animals in a caravan (qiṭār). The passage covers several scenarios. First, if a person drives an animal and the saddle falls on and kills someone, the diyah is on the 'āqilah (tribal guarantors) because the driver is negligent. Second, if a person leads a caravan and a camel tramples a man, the leader's 'āqilah owes diyah because the leader could have prevented it. If there is also a driver (sā'iq), both are liable equally because both have control. However, if the camel is ridden, the rider is a direct agent (mubāshir) and he alone is liable for damages caused by trampling. Third, if a person ties an extra camel to a caravan without the leader's knowledge, and that camel kills someone, the leader's 'āqilah initially pays diyah, but they then recover from the 'āqilah of the one who tied the camel, because the leader's neglect is the immediate cause but the real cause is the tying. The passage also includes a brief query about a camel that eats from a field and is imprisoned, with the cat's leg broken – the author notes that the opinion is for the judge to decide. The text ends abruptly with 'They said: This is if...' indicating possible continuation.

Key Terms
عاقلة (‘āqilah): The tribal group or relatives who pay diyah on behalf of the killer in cases of accidental killing.
دية (diyah): Blood-money paid for killing, in this context due to accidental death caused by an animal.
قطار (qiṭār): A caravan of camels tied in a line.
تسبب (tasabbub): Causation; being an indirect cause of harm, as opposed to direct perpetration (mubāsharah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man ties his camel to a caravan without the leader's knowledge, and the camel kills someone, who pays the diyah initially?
A) The owner of the camel
B) The leader of the caravan's 'āqilah
C) The man who tied the camel
D) The driver of the caravan

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for accidental killing by an animal attaches to the person who controls or fails to control it, with diyah paid by their 'āqilah.

Liability of the Leader and Driver in Caravan Accidents
Ruling with causal explanation on liability for caravan accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct action (mubasharah) and indirect causation (tasabbub) in Hanafi fiqh and apply the ruling that the 'aqila of the leader and driver are liable for diya when a caravan animal kills someone.

Core Idea
The leader's duty to safeguard the caravan makes him liable via tasabbub; the rider's direct control makes him liable via mubasharah.

In Hanafi tort law, liability for harm caused by animals depends on the role of the person in control. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that when a caravan (qitar) animal tramples a person, the leader (qa'id) and driver (sa'iq) are liable because they failed in their duty of care, while the rider (rakb) is solely liable for harm caused directly by his mount. Understanding this distinction between indirect causation and direct action is essential for applying diya rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
The passage discusses three roles: the leader who walks in front of the caravan, the driver who walks behind, and the rider who sits on the animal. Each has a different relationship to the animal and thus different liability.
The leader and driver are responsible for the entire caravan; the rider controls a single animal.
2
Classify the harmful act as direct or indirect
When a camel tramples a person, the rider's act is mubasharah (direct) because he is on the animal and can control it. The leader's act is tasabbub (indirect causation) because his negligence in guarding the caravan allowed the accident to happen.
The text states: 'فإن ذلك ضمانه على الراكب وحده لأنه جعل فيه مباشراً'.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for diya payment
For indirect causation, diya is paid by the 'aqila (male paternal relatives) of the negligent person. For direct action, the diya is on the individual himself.
The text: 'ضمان النفس على العاقلة وضمان المال عليه في ماله'.
4
Note the effect of multiple parties
If both leader and driver are present, they share liability equally because both had the duty to guard the caravan. The rider's liability is separate and falls solely on him.
The text: 'وإن كان معه سائق فعليهما'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك

رجل فقتله ضمن وإن قاد قطاراً فوطئ بعير إنساناً ضمن عاقلة القائد الدية وإن كان معه سائق فعليهما وإن ربط بعيراً على قطار رجع على عاقلة القائد بدية ما تلف به على عاقلة عبده وكان لمولى البادئ أن يدفع إليه العبد المدفوع ثانياً إليه عن حقه فلا يفيده الدفع وإنما دفع أرش شجة اللاحق لأنه متى دفع أرش عبد اللاحق فقد طهر البادئ عن الجناية وصار كأنه لم يجن وإنما جنى عليه العبد اللاحق فيخاطب مولى اللاحق بالدفع والفداء وأي ذلك اختار لا يبقى لواحد منهما على صاحبه سبيل لأنه وصل إلى كل واحد منهما حقه. وإن أبي مولى البادئ أن يدفع الأرش فلا شيء له في عتق الآخر فإن مولى البادئ كان مخيراً بين العفو وبين دفع الأرش والمطالبة شجة لعبده، فإذا امتنع من دفع الأرش صار مختاراً للعفو وصار كأنه قال عفوتك عن حقي فيبطل حقه. ولو مات اللاحق وبقي البادئ خير ،مولاه، فإن دفعه بطل حقه، وإن فداه بأرش عبده في الفداء لأن البادئ طاهر عن الجناية لعفو وأحدهما عن جناية نصف العبد ولا يزداد حقه فكذا هذا .
سبب للضمان غيره
قال رحمه الله : (ولو) ساق دابة فوقع السرج على رجل فقتله ضمن) يعني إذا ساق دابة ولها سرج فوقع السرج على رجل فقتله ضمن عاقلته الدية وقد قدمناها بفروعها. قال رحمه الله : (وإن قاد قطاراً فوطئ بعير إنساناً ضمن عاقلة القائد الدية لأن القائد عليه حفظ القطار كالسائق وقد أمكنه التحرز عنه فصار متعدياً بالتقصير فيه والتسبب بلفظ التعدي أن ضمان النفس على العاقلة وضمان المال عليه في ماله. رجل له مزرعة فأكلها جمل غير فأخذه وحبسه في الأصطبل ثم وجد الجمل مكسور الرجل كيف الحكم بينهما في ذلك ؟ فقال : إن لم يكسر رجله في حبسه قالوا لا ضمان عليه وقد قالوا الضمان عليه ما لم يسلمه إلى صاحبه والرأي فيه إلى القاضي قال رحمه الله : (وإن كان معه سائق فعليهما) أي إذا كان مع القائد سائق تجب على عاقلتهما الضمان لاستوائهما في التسبب لأن قائد الواحد قائد الكل، وكذا سائقه لاتصال اللازمة، وأما البعير الذي هو راكبه فهو ضامن لما أصابه فيجب عليه وعلى القائد غير ما أصابه بالإيطاء فإن ذلك ضمانه على الراكب وحده لأنه جعل فيه مباشراً حتى جرت عليه أحكام المباشرة على ما بيناه.
Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury upon Them, etc. A man... he killed him, so he is liable. And if he led a caravan and a camel stepped on a person, the 'aqila of the leader are liable for the diya. And if there is a driver with him, then both are liable. And if he tied a camel to a caravan, he may recourse against the 'aqila of the leader for the diya of what was destroyed by it, against the 'aqila of the bondman. And the master of the first could give to him the slave given secondly to him for his right, but the giving does not benefit him; rather he gave the blood-money of the wound of the latter because when he gives the blood-money of the slave of the latter, the first becomes innocent of the injury and becomes as if he did not injure, and it is as if the latter slave injured, so the master of the latter is addressed with surrender and ransom, and whichever he chooses, there remains no way for one of them against the other because each has received his right. And if the master of the first refuses to give the blood-money, then he has no claim in the emancipation of the other, for the master of the first had the choice between forgiveness and giving the blood-money and claiming the wound of his slave; so when he refrains from giving the blood-money, he becomes the one who chose forgiveness and becomes as if he said, 'I forgive you for my right,' so his right is void. And if the latter dies and the first remains, his master is given choice: if he surrenders him, his right is void; if he ransoms him with the blood-money of his slave in the ransom, because the first is innocent of the injury due to forgiveness and one of them is innocent of the injury of half the slave, and his right does not increase. So this is so. A cause for liability other than him. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he led an animal and the saddle fell on a man and killed him, he is liable' meaning if he led an animal that has a saddle, and the saddle fell on a man and killed him, his 'aqila are liable for the diya, and we have already presented it with its branches. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he led a caravan and a camel stepped on a person, the 'aqila of the leader are liable for the diya' because the leader is responsible for guarding the caravan like the driver, and he could have been cautious about it, so he becomes a transgressor by negligence in it and causation. By the word 'transgression', the liability for life is on the 'aqila and liability for property is on his own wealth. A man had a farm; a camel of someone else ate it; he took it and confined it in the stable, then found the camel with a broken leg: how is the judgment between them? He said: if he did not break its leg in his confinement, they said no liability; but they also said liability is on him as long as he does not surrender it to its owner, and the decision is left to the judge. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if there is a driver with him, then both are liable' meaning if the leader has a driver, the liability is on both their 'aqilas due to their equality in causation, because the leader of one is the leader of all, and likewise his driver due to connection of necessity. As for the camel that is its rider, he is liable for what it hits, so it is obligatory on him and on the leader for what it hit by trampling; for that liability is on the rider alone because he is made a direct actor, so the rulings of direct action apply to him, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with causal explanation from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It opens by stating that the 'aqila of the leader of a caravan are liable for diya when a camel in the caravan tramples a person to death. The key legal principle is that the leader has a duty to safeguard the caravan; his failure to exercise caution makes him a transgressor (mut'addi) through negligence, and his act is classified as causation (tasabbub). The commentary then clarifies that if a driver (sa'iq) is also present, both leader and driver share liability equally because both are equally responsible for guarding the animals. The commentator also distinguishes between the rider of a camel: if the rider is on the camel, he is a direct actor (mubashir) and bears sole liability for harm caused by the camel's trampling, because he could directly control it. The passage also includes a brief aside about a man who impounds a camel that ate his crop; if the camel's leg is broken while in his custody, liability depends on whether he caused the breakage. The overall reasoning is based on the principle of causation and direct action.

Key Terms
قطار (qitar): a caravan or string of pack animals tied together in a line
قائد (qa'id): the person who leads the caravan from the front
سائق (sa'iq): the person who drives the caravan from behind
عاقلة ('aqila): the male paternal relatives of the offender who collectively pay diya in cases of accidental killing
تسبب (tasabbub): causation; indirect action leading to harm, as opposed to direct action (mubasharah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person leads a caravan (qitar) and also has a driver (sa'iq), and a camel steps on a man, who is liable for the diya?
A) Only the leader
B) Only the driver
C) Both the leader and the driver
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, indirect causation (tasabbub) in caravan accidents places diya on the 'aqila of the leader and driver, while direct action (mubasharah) places it on the individual.

Liability for Impounding a Damaging Camel
Applied ruling on liability for injury to impounded animal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a person who impounds an animal that caused damage is liable for injury to the animal only if he directly causes it, and otherwise is not liable.

Core Idea
If you impound a trespassing camel and its leg is broken, you are liable only if you broke it; otherwise, you must return it without liability.

This lesson explores a secondary ruling from the same passage: a man's crop is eaten by a stray camel. He impounds the camel, then finds it with a broken leg. The Hanafi scholars rule that if he did not break the leg, he is not liable for the injury, but he must still return it to its owner. However, if he caused the break, he is liable. This illustrates the principle that liability follows direct causation (mubasharah) even in property torts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial wrong: the camel trespassing
The camel ate the man's crops without permission, which is a wrong. The owner of the crop has a right to compensation.
The text does not detail the initial liability; it focuses on the impoundment.
2
The impoundment action
The man takes the camel and confines it in a stable, presumably to secure compensation or force the owner to pay.
Impounding is permissible but carries duties.
3
Determine the cause of the broken leg
Two scenarios: either the leg was broken during confinement (direct act of the man) or it was already broken or broken by other cause.
The judge will assess evidence.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule on liability
If the man directly broke the leg (mubasharah), he is liable for the value of the camel or the injury. If he did not, he is not liable, but he must still return the camel.
The text explicitly says: 'إن لم يكسر رجله في حبسه قالوا لا ضمان عليه'.
5
Note the special rule about returning
Even if not liable, the man cannot keep the camel indefinitely. The judge may decide on compensation for the crop.
The text adds: 'الضمان عليه ما لم يسلمه إلى صاحبه' meaning he is liable for the camel until he returns it.

Book of Diyat, Chapter on Injury by Animals and Injury upon Them, etc. A man... he killed him, so he is liable. And if he led a caravan and a camel stepped on a person, the 'aqila of the leader are liable for the diya. And if there is a driver with him, then both are liable. And if he tied a camel to a caravan, he may recourse against the 'aqila of the leader for the diya of what was destroyed by it, against the 'aqila of the bondman. And the master of the first could give to him the slave given secondly to him for his right, but the giving does not benefit him; rather he gave the blood-money of the wound of the latter because when he gives the blood-money of the slave of the latter, the first becomes innocent of the injury and becomes as if he did not injure, and it is as if the latter slave injured, so the master of the latter is addressed with surrender and ransom, and whichever he chooses, there remains no way for one of them against the other because each has received his right. And if the master of the first refuses to give the blood-money, then he has no claim in the emancipation of the other, for the master of the first had the choice between forgiveness and giving the blood-money and claiming the wound of his slave; so when he refrains from giving the blood-money, he becomes the one who chose forgiveness and becomes as if he said, 'I forgive you for my right,' so his right is void. And if the latter dies and the first remains, his master is given choice: if he surrenders him, his right is void; if he ransoms him with the blood-money of his slave in the ransom, because the first is innocent of the injury due to forgiveness and one of them is innocent of the injury of half the slave, and his right does not increase. So this is so. A cause for liability other than him. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he led an animal and the saddle fell on a man and killed him, he is liable' meaning if he led an animal that has a saddle, and the saddle fell on a man and killed him, his 'aqila are liable for the diya, and we have already presented it with its branches. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he led a caravan and a camel stepped on a person, the 'aqila of the leader are liable for the diya' because the leader is responsible for guarding the caravan like the driver, and he could have been cautious about it, so he becomes a transgressor by negligence in it and causation. By the word 'transgression', the liability for life is on the 'aqila and liability for property is on his own wealth. A man had a farm; a camel of someone else ate it; he took it and confined it in the stable, then found the camel with a broken leg: how is the judgment between them? He said: if he did not break its leg in his confinement, they said no liability; but they also said liability is on him as long as he does not surrender it to its owner, and the decision is left to the judge. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if there is a driver with him, then both are liable' meaning if the leader has a driver, the liability is on both their 'aqilas due to their equality in causation, because the leader of one is the leader of all, and likewise his driver due to connection of necessity. As for the camel that is its rider, he is liable for what it hits, so it is obligatory on him and on the leader for what it hit by trampling; for that liability is on the rider alone because he is made a direct actor, so the rulings of direct action apply to him, as we have explained.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with causal explanation from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. It opens by stating that the 'aqila of the leader of a caravan are liable for diya when a camel in the caravan tramples a person to death. The key legal principle is that the leader has a duty to safeguard the caravan; his failure to exercise caution makes him a transgressor (mut'addi) through negligence, and his act is classified as causation (tasabbub). The commentary then clarifies that if a driver (sa'iq) is also present, both leader and driver share liability equally because both are equally responsible for guarding the animals. The commentator also distinguishes between the rider of a camel: if the rider is on the camel, he is a direct actor (mubashir) and bears sole liability for harm caused by the camel's trampling, because he could directly control it. The passage also includes a brief aside about a man who impounds a camel that ate his crop; if the camel's leg is broken while in his custody, liability depends on whether he caused the breakage. The overall reasoning is based on the principle of causation and direct action.

Key Terms
قطار (qitar): a caravan or string of pack animals tied together in a line
قائد (qa'id): the person who leads the caravan from the front
سائق (sa'iq): the person who drives the caravan from behind
عاقلة ('aqila): the male paternal relatives of the offender who collectively pay diya in cases of accidental killing
تسبب (tasabbub): causation; indirect action leading to harm, as opposed to direct action (mubasharah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man's crop is eaten by a camel. He impounds the camel. The camel is found with a broken leg. When is the man liable for the broken leg?
A) Always, because he had custody
B) Only if he directly broke the leg
C) Never, because the camel trespassed
D) Only if the owner demands compensation

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, liability for injury to impounded property follows direct causation, but custodial responsibility may still require return.

Liability for a Tied Camel: Leader's Duty and Recourse
Details the liability and recourse when a stranger ties his camel to a moving caravan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on initial liability when a stranger ties his camel to a caravan, apply the distinction between moving and stationary scenarios, and explain when the leader may seek recourse from the tier's 'aqila.

Core Idea
Caravan leader initially liable, but may recourse to tier if unaware of tying.

Imagine you are leading a caravan, and someone secretly ties his camel to the back. The camel steps on a person. Who pays the blood money? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explains the Hanafi view: the leader is initially liable because he failed to protect his caravan. However, if he was unaware of the tying, he can seek reimbursement from the tier's 'aqila through istihsan (juristic preference). This lesson unpacks that ruling and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and ruling
State the basic case: a man ties his camel to a caravan without the leader's knowledge; the tied camel kills a person. The Hanafi ruling is that the leader's 'aqila (tribal surety group) pays the diya (blood money) initially.
The leader is considered a 'causer' (musabbib) due to negligence in safeguarding.
2
Analyze the distinction between moving and stationary caravan
Note that the commentary differentiates: if the caravan was moving when tied, the leader is liable but can seek recourse if unaware. If stationary, the leader cannot seek recourse because he consented by moving the caravan after the tying.
This distinction comes from nawadir (rare transmissions).
3
Understand the role of istihsan
Explain that the default qiyas (analogy) would not allow recourse because ignorance does not negate causation. However, istihsan permits recourse to prevent the tier from unjustly escaping liability.
Istihsan is a key Hanafi source of law that sometimes overrides qiyas.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Compare the position of Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir (does not distinguish moving/stationary; recourse allowed if unaware in both cases) with that in nawadir (distinction based on movement).
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is from al-Jami' al-Saghir and istihsan.
5
Apply the ruling to related scenarios
Consider the cases of multiple parties (rider, driver, leader) and the prodding case. Observe how the principles of mubashir (direct actor) and musabbib (causer) interact.
The commentator questions the prodding case due to inconsistency with the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وإن ربط بعيراً على قطار رجع على عاقلة القائد بدية ما تلف به على عاقلة الرابط) أي إذا ربط رجل بعيراً على قطار والقائد لذلك القطار لا يعلم فوطئ البعير المربوط إنساناً فقتله فعلى عاقلة القائد ديته لأنه يمكنه أن يصون قطاره عن ربط غيره به فإذا ترك صيانته صار متعدياً بالتقصير وهو متسبب وفيه الدية على العاقلة كما في قتل الخطأ يرجعون بها على عاقلة الرابط لأنه هو الذي أوقعهم فيه، وإنما لا يجب الضمان على القائد
ثم
والرابط ابتداء مع أن كل واحد منهما متسبب لأن القود بمنزلة المباشرة بالنسبة إلى الربط لاتصال التلف به دون الربط فيجب فيه الضمان وحده ثم يرجع به عليه. قالوا : هذا إذا الرابط ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان سائقها فما أصابت في فورها ضمن وإن أرسل طيراً أو كلباً ولم يكن سائقاً أو انفلتت دابته فأصابت مالاً أو آدمياً ليلاً أو نهاراً لا يضمن وفي فقء

ربط والقطار يسير لأن الرابط أمر بالقود دلالة وإذا لم يعلم لا يمكنه التحفظ عنه ولكن جهله لا ينفي وجوب الضمان عليه لتحقق الإتلاف منه وإنما ينفي الإثم فيكون قرار الضمان على الرابط، وأما إذا ربط والإبل واقفة ضمنها عاقلة القائد ولا يرجعون على عاقلة الرابط بما لحقهم من الضمان لأن القائد رضي بذلك والتلف قد اتصل بفعله فلا وهو القياس فيما إذا لم يعلم لأن الجهل لا ينافي التسبب ولا الضمان إلا أنا استحسنا الرجوع لما ذكرنا وفي الجامع الصغير : رجل قاد قطاراً في طريق المسلمين فجاء وربطه والقائد لا يعلم به أو علم فأصاب ذلك البعير إنساناً فضمانه على القائد دون الرابط وإن كان كل واحد منهما متسبباً للإتلاف وهل يرجع على عاقلة الرابط؟ إن علم لا يرجع، وإن لم يعلم يرجع ولم يفصل محمد رحمه الله في الجامع الصغير بين ما إذا ربط البعير بالقطار والقطار يسير وفي بعض كتب النوادر : وإن كان القطار لا حالة الربط يسير فقادها القائد بعد الربط لا يرجع القائد على عاقلة الرابط، علم القائد بربطه أو لم يعلم. وإن حالة الربط فالقائد يرجع على عاقلة الرابط إذا لم يعلم بربطه . وفي المنتقى : وإذا سار الرجل على دابته وخلفه رديف وخلف الدابة سائق وأمامها قائد فوطئت إنساناً فالدية عليهم أرباعاً وعلى الراكب والرديف الكفارة، وإذا سار الرجل على دابته في الطريق فعثرت بحجر وضعه رجل أو قد كان بناه رجل أو بماء قد صبه رجل فوقعت على إنسان وأتلفته فالضمان على الذي وضع الحجر في المكان أو الماء لأنه مسبب في هذا الإتلاف
كان القطار يسير .
صب
وهو متعد في هذا السبب ولا ضمان على الراكب قالوا ولو نخس الدابة رجل فوطئت إنساناً فالضمان عليهما إن وطئت في فور النخس لأن الموت حصل بثقل الراكب وفعل الناخس فيكون مضافاً إليهما أقول : ولقائل أن يقول الراكب مباشر فيما أتلفت بالوطء لحصول التلف بثقله وثقل الدابة جميعاً كما صرحوا به والناخس مسبب كما مر في الكتاب ، وإذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب فالإضافة إلى المباشر أولى كما صرحوا به لا سيما في مسألة الراكب والسائق فما بالهم صرحوا هنا بإضافة الفعل إلى الراكب والناخس معاً وحكموا بوجوب الدية عليهما جميعاً فتدبره
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he tied a camel to a caravan, he may recourse against the 'aqila of the leader for the diya of what was destroyed by it, against the 'aqila of the one who tied.' Meaning if a man tied a camel to a caravan, and the leader of that caravan does not know, then the tied camel steps on a person and kills him, then the diya is on the 'aqila of the leader because he could protect his caravan from someone tying to it; if he abandons that protection, he becomes a transgressor by negligence, and he is a causer, and the diya is on the 'aqila as in accidental killing; they may then have recourse for it against the 'aqila of the one who tied because he is the one who involved them. And the liability does not fall initially on both the leader and the tier even though each is a causer, because the leading is like direct action relative to the tying, due to the connection of the loss to it rather than to the tying, so liability is on him alone, then he has recourse. They said: This is if the one who tied... [text unclear] And if he sent an animal and was its driver, then whatever it hits in its initial impulse he is liable. And if he sent a bird or dog and was not a driver, or his animal escaped and it hit property or a person night or day, he is not liable. [part about a camel tied while caravan is moving] Because the tier is commanded by the lead impliedly, and if he does not know, he cannot guard against it, but his ignorance does not negate the obligation of liability due to the actual destruction from him; it only negates sin, so the liability rests on the tier. But if he tied while the camels were standing, then the 'aqila of the leader is liable, and they do not have recourse against the 'aqila of the tier for the liability that befell them because the leader consented to that, and the loss was connected to his action, so no; and this is the analogy when he does not know, because ignorance does not negate causation or liability, but we have exercised istihsan for the recourse because of what we mentioned. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man led a caravan on the Muslims' road; someone came and tied (a camel) to it, and the leader does not know it, or knows, then that camel hits a person, the liability is on the leader not the tier, even though each is a causer for the destruction. Does he have recourse against the 'aqila of the tier? If he knew, he does not have recourse; if he did not know, he does have recourse. And Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, did not distinguish in al-Jami' al-Saghir between whether the tie occurred while the caravan was moving or not. And in some books of nawadir: If the caravan was not moving at the time of tying, and the leader led it after the tying, then the leader does not have recourse against the 'aqila of the tier, whether the leader knew of his tying or not. And if (the caravan) was moving at the time of tying, then the leader has recourse against the 'aqila of the tier if he did not know of his tying. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man rides his animal with a rear-sitter behind him, and behind the animal is a driver, and in front is a leader, and it steps on a person, then the diya is on all of them by quarters, and the rider and the rear-sitter must also give kaffarah. And if a man rides his animal on the road, and it stumbles over a stone placed by a man, or over a structure built by a man, or over water poured by a man, and falls on a person and kills him, then liability is on the one who placed the stone or water, because he is a causer in this destruction, and he is a transgressor in this cause, and there is no liability on the rider. They said: And if a man prods (nakhasa) an animal and it steps on a person, then liability is on both of them if the stepping occurs in the impulse of the prodding, because death occurs due to the rider's weight and the action of the proder, so it is attributed to both. I say: It may be said that the rider is a direct actor in what is destroyed by stepping, because destruction occurs by his weight and the weight of the animal together, as they stated, and the proder is a causer as mentioned in the book. And when direct actor and causer meet, attribution to the direct actor is primary, as they stated, especially in the case of the rider and driver; so why did they state here that the act is attributed to both rider and proder together and ruled that diya is obligatory on both? So ponder it.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a complex ruling about liability when a person ties his camel to a moving caravan without the leader's knowledge. The commentary explains that the leader of the caravan is primarily liable because he failed to protect his caravan. However, since the tier caused the situation, the leader's 'aqila can seek recourse from the tier's 'aqila. The text distinguishes three scenarios: (1) If the caravan was moving when the camel was tied, the leader is liable but can seek recourse if he was unaware. (2) If the caravan was stationary at the time of tying, the leader is liable and cannot seek recourse because he consented by moving after tying. (3) If the caravan was stationary and the leader later moves it, he cannot seek recourse regardless of knowledge. The commentary also cites al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Muntaqa for related scenarios including multiple parties (rider, rear-sitter, driver, leader) sharing liability. The final part discusses the case where someone prods an animal: the rider and proder share liability if the animal steps in the immediate aftermath, but the commentator questions this because normally a direct actor (rider) should bear sole liability. This is a point of intra-Hanafi discussion.

Key Terms
ربط (rabt): the act of tying one's animal to another's caravan
رابط (rabit): the person who ties his animal
استحسان (istihsan): juristic preference; a departure from qiyas for a stronger reason
مباشر (mubashir): direct actor who directly causes the harm
متسبب (musabbib): causer who indirectly causes the harm through a chain of events
عاقلة ('aqila): tribal surety group responsible for paying diya on behalf of a member

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man ties his camel to a moving caravan without the leader's knowledge, and the camel kills a person, what is the initial liability and can the leader seek recourse?
A) The tier's 'aqila pays the diya and cannot seek recourse
B) The leader's 'aqila pays the diya and can seek recourse from the tier's 'aqila if the leader was unaware
C) The leader's 'aqila pays the diya and cannot seek recourse under any circumstances
D) Both 'aqilas share the diya equally

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a caravan leader is initially liable for harm caused by a tied camel, but if the leader was unaware, he may seek recourse from the tier's 'aqila to ensure the ultimate causer bears the loss.

Sending Animals, Birds, and Dogs: Liability Rules
Distinguishes liability when sending an animal with or without a driver, and for birds/dogs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for harm caused by an animal, bird, or dog, distinguishing between when the person is a driver and when not.

Core Idea
Liability for animal harm depends on active driving; no liability for birds or escaped animals.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi tort law: whether the person is actively driving the animal or merely releasing it. The ruling hinges on the concept of 'fawr' (initial impulse) and the possibility of control. We will examine the two scenarios and the rationale behind each.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two distinct scenarios
The matn gives two rulings: (1) when the person is a driver (sa'iq) and (2) when he is not (bird/dog/escaped animal). In the first case, liability for harm in the initial impulse; in the second, no liability.
Focus on the contrast between 'sending with driving' and 'sending without driving'.
2
Define 'fawr' (initial impulse)
Liability is limited to the immediate aftermath of sending/driving. The term 'fawr' specifies that only harm occurring directly from the act of driving is compensable, not subsequent independent acts.
This restricts liability temporally.
3
Analyze the talil (reason) for birds
The commentary explains that birds cannot be driven because their bodies do not bear driving. Hence, the absence of control nullifies liability. The same reasoning applies to dogs unless actively herded.
The illah (cause) is the impossibility of sawq.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical
Test your understanding: If a person opens a gate for a horse without riding or driving it, and the horse runs off and damages property, is he liable? Answer: No, because he was not a driver.
Practice distinguishing driving from mere release.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان سائقها فما أصابت في فورها ضمن يعني إذا أرسل إنسان بهيمة وساقها فكل شيء أصابته في فورها فإنه يضمنه .
قال رحمه الله : (وإن أرسل طيراً أو كلباً ولم يكن سائقاً أو انفلتت دابته فأصابت مالاً أو آدمياً ليلاً أو نهاراً لا يضمن أي في هذه الصور كلها ؛ أما الطير فلأن بدنه لا يحتمل السوق فصار وجود السوق وعدمه سواء فلا يضمن مطلقاً بخلاف الدابة فإن بدنها يحمل السوق
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And whoever sent an animal and was its driver, then whatever it hits in its initial impulse, he is liable.' Meaning if a person sends an animal and drives it, then everything it hits in its initial impulse, he is liable for it. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'And if he sent a bird or a dog and was not a driver, or his animal escaped and it hit property or a person, night or day, he is not liable.' Meaning in all these forms; as for the bird, because its body cannot bear driving, so the existence of driving and its absence are the same, so he is not liable absolutely, unlike the animal, for its body can bear driving.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents two clear rulings from the matn. The first ruling: if a person sends (releases) an animal and also acts as its driver (i.e., he actively drives it from behind), then he is liable for any harm caused by the animal in its initial impulse (fawr), meaning the immediate aftermath of being sent. The reason is that he is a direct actor in causing the animal to move. The second ruling: if he sends a bird or a dog without being a driver (i.e., he just releases them without herding), or if his domestic animal escapes (unleashed), then he is not liable for any harm they cause, whether to property or persons, day or night. The commentary explains the rationale: for birds, their bodies cannot be driven in the same way as a quadruped, so the presence or absence of driving makes no difference; they are not subject to control, so liability does not attach. For dogs, likewise, unless the person is actively directing them (e.g., hunting). For escaped domestic animals, the owner is not liable because he did not cause the escape or control the animal. The distinction hinges on whether the person is actively driving (sa'iq) or not.

Key Terms
فور (fawr): the initial impulse or immediate aftermath of sending/driving an animal
سوق (sawq): the act of driving an animal from behind
سائق (sa'iq): the driver who impels the animal forward

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person releases a bird without driving it, and the bird injures someone, is the person liable?
A) Yes, because the bird is his property
B) No, because a bird cannot be driven and the person was not a driver
C) Yes, if the injury occurs at night
D) No, only if the bird is a trained hunting bird

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for animal harm requires active driving; without it, there is no liability for birds, dogs, or escaped animals.

Liability for Sending Animals: The Role of Driving
Commentary on matn: ruling on animal injury liability with istihsan justification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for liability when sending an animal and explain why driving (sa'iq) is a condition, using istihsan.

Core Idea
Driving an animal makes you liable for its injuries in the initial rush; without driving, the animal's voluntary act is its own.

This lesson unpacks the core ruling from the matn: a person who sends an animal and drives it is liable for any harm it causes immediately after release. The commentary explains why driving is essential, using istihsan to override the general principle that voluntary acts are attributed to the doer. We will also examine the special cases of birds and dogs, and the intra-Hanafi dispute on dog liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is 'sending an animal' (irsal al-bahimah). The ruling: if the sender is also the driver (sa'iq), he is liable for injuries occurring in the initial rush (fawr).
'Initial rush' means immediately after sending, before the animal stops.
2
Distinguish animals based on capacity for driving
Birds cannot be driven, so sending a bird never creates liability. Beasts can be driven, so driving must be proven. Dogs can be driven, but if no actual or constructive driving occurs, the sender is not liable.
Constructive driving means the dog injures something in the very act of being sent.
3
Understand the istihsan justification
Normally, a voluntary act is attributed to the one who acts (the animal). By istihsan, we attribute the animal's act to the driver to protect lives and property, overriding qiyas.
Istihsan is a form of juristic preference unique to Hanafi methodology.
4
Apply the rule to the dog case
If a dog is sent without being driven and attacks someone, the sender is not liable. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf differs, but the fatwa is on Muhammad's position.
Hunting is an exception: the dog's act is attributed to the sender due to necessity.

Source Text

كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك
الرابط ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان سائقها فما أصابت في فورها ضمن وإن أرسل طيراً أو كلباً ولم يكن سائقاً أو انفلتت دابته فأصابت مالاً أو آدمياً ليلاً أو نهاراً لا يضمن وفي فقء به يرجع بعير آخر ربط والقطار يسير لأن الرابط أمر بالقود دلالة وإذا لم يعلم لا يمكنه التحفظ عنه ولكن جهله لا ينفي وجوب الضمان عليه لتحقق الإتلاف منه وإنما ينفي الإثم فيكون قرار الضمان على الرابط، وأما إذا ربط والإبل واقفة ضمنها عاقلة القائد ولا يرجعون على عاقلة الرابط بما لحقهم من الضمان لأن القائد رضي بذلك والتلف قد اتصل بفعله فلا وهو القياس فيما إذا لم يعلم لأن الجهل لا ينافي التسبب ولا الضمان إلا أنا استحسنا الرجوع لما ذكرنا وفي الجامع الصغير : رجل قاد قطاراً في طريق المسلمين فجاء وربطه والقائد لا يعلم به أو علم فأصاب ذلك البعير إنساناً فضمانه على القائد دون الرابط وإن كان كل واحد منهما متسبباً للإتلاف وهل يرجع على عاقلة الرابط؟ إن علم لا يرجع، وإن لم يعلم يرجع ولم يفصل محمد رحمه الله في الجامع الصغير بين ما إذا ربط البعير بالقطار والقطار يسير وفي بعض كتب النوادر : وإن كان القطار لا حالة الربط يسير فقادها القائد بعد الربط لا يرجع القائد على عاقلة الرابط، علم القائد بربطه أو لم يعلم. وإن حالة الربط فالقائد يرجع على عاقلة الرابط إذا لم يعلم بربطه . وفي المنتقى : وإذا سار الرجل على دابته وخلفه رديف وخلف الدابة سائق وأمامها قائد فوطئت إنساناً فالدية عليهم أرباعاً وعلى الراكب والرديف الكفارة، وإذا سار الرجل على دابته في الطريق فعثرت بحجر وضعه رجل أو قد كان بناه رجل أو بماء قد صبه رجل فوقعت على إنسان وأتلفته فالضمان على الذي وضع الحجر في المكان أو الماء لأنه مسبب في هذا الإتلاف
وهو متعد في هذا السبب ولا ضمان على الراكب قالوا ولو نخس الدابة رجل فوطئت إنساناً فالضمان عليهما إن وطئت في فور النخس لأن الموت حصل بثقل الراكب وفعل الناخس فيكون مضافاً إليهما أقول : ولقائل أن يقول الراكب مباشر فيما أتلفت بالوطء لحصول التلف بثقله وثقل الدابة جميعاً كما صرحوا به والناخس مسبب كما مر في الكتاب ، وإذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب فالإضافة إلى المباشر أولى كما صرحوا به لا سيما في مسألة الراكب والسائق فما بالهم صرحوا هنا بإضافة الفعل إلى الراكب والناخس معاً وحكموا بوجوب الدية عليهما جميعاً فتدبره قال رحمه الله: (ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان سائقها فما أصابت في فورها ضمن يعني إذا أرسل إنسان بهيمة وساقها فكل شيء أصابته في فورها فإنه يضمنه .
قال رحمه الله : (وإن أرسل طيراً أو كلباً ولم يكن سائقاً أو انفلتت دابته فأصابت مالاً أو آدمياً ليلاً أو نهاراً لا يضمن أي في هذه الصور كلها ؛ أما الطير فلأن بدنه لا يحتمل السوق فصار وجود السوق وعدمه سواء فلا يضمن مطلقاً بخلاف الدابة فإن بدنها يحمل السوق
فيعتبر فيها السوق ومن ثم قالوا ولو أرسل بازياً في الحرم فقتل لا يضمن المرسل، وأما الكلب فلأنه وإن كان يحتمل السوق لكنه لم يوجد منه السوق حقيقة بأن يمشي خلفه ولا حكماً بأن يصيب على فور الإرسال والتعدي يكون بالسوق فلا يضمن، وهذا لأن الأصل أن الفعل الاختياري يضاف إلى فعل صاحبه ولا يجوز إضافته إلى غيره لأنا تركنا ذلك في فعل البهمية إذا وجد منه السوق فأضفناه إليه استحساناً صيانة للأنفس والأموال، وإذا لم يوجد منه السوق بقي على الأصل ولا يجوز إضافته إليه لعدم الفعل منه مباشرة وتسبباً بخلاف ما إذا أرسل الكلب على صيد حيث يؤكل ما أصابه وإن لم يكن سائقاً له حقيقة ولا حكماً لأن الحاجة مست إلى الاصطياد به فأضيف إلى المرسل ما دام الكلب في تلك الجهة ولم يفتر عنها إذ لا طريق للاصطياد سواه، وهذا لأن الاصطياد به مشروع ولو شرط السوق لاستد بابه وهو مفتوح فأضيف إليه، ولو غاب عن بصره مع الصيد ولا حاجة إليه في حق ضمان العدوان فبقي على الأصل فكان مضافاً إلى الكلب لأنه مختار في فعله ولا يصلح المرسل فلا يضاف فعله إلى غيره.
The book of diyat, chapter of injury by an animal and injury upon it and other things. The one who ties: And whoever sends an animal and is its driver, then what it injures in its initial rush he is liable. And if he sends a bird or a dog and is not a driver, or his animal escapes and it injures property or a person by night or day, he is not liable. And in [the case of] a camel tied to a moving caravan, because the tier implicitly commands the leading, and if he does not know, he cannot guard against it, but his ignorance does not negate the obligation of liability upon him due to the actual destruction from him, but it negates sin, so the liability rests on the tier. But if he ties while the camels are standing, the clan of the leader is liable, and they do not seek recourse from the clan of the tier for the liability that befell them because the leader consented to that and the destruction was connected to his act. That is the analogy when he does not know, because ignorance does not preclude causation nor liability, but we practiced istihsan for recourse as we mentioned. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man led a caravan in the path of the Muslims, then someone came and tied [an animal to it] while the leader did not know or knew, and that camel injured a person, so the liability is on the leader, not the tier, though each is a cause of the destruction. And does he have recourse against the clan of the tier? If he knew, no recourse; if he did not know, he has recourse. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) did not distinguish in al-Jami' al-Saghir between whether the camel was tied while the caravan was moving. And in some books of al-Nawadir: If the caravan was not moving at the time of tying, and the leader led after the tying, the leader has no recourse against the clan of the tier, whether he knew of the tying or not. And if the caravan was moving, the leader has recourse against the clan of the tier if he did not know of the tying. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man rides his animal and behind him is a rear rider, and behind the animal is a driver, and in front of it is a leader, and it tramples a person, the blood money is upon them in quarters, and upon the rider and the rear rider is the expiation. And if a man rides his animal in the road and it stumbles over a stone placed by a man or a wall built by a man or water poured by a man, and it falls on a person and destroys him, the liability is upon the one who placed the stone or the water, because he is a cause in this destruction, and he is transgressing in this cause, and there is no liability on the rider. They said: And if a man prods an animal and it tramples a person, the liability is on both if the trampling occurred in the initial rush of the prodding, because death occurred by the weight of the rider and the act of the prodder, so it is attributed to both. I say: One could say the rider is a direct agent in what is destroyed by trampling because the destruction occurs by his weight and the animal's weight together, as they stated, and the prodder is a cause, as previously in the book. And when direct agent and cause are combined, attribution to the direct agent is preferable, as they stated, especially in the case of the rider and the driver. So why did they state here to attribute the act to both the rider and the prodder and rule that the blood money is obligatory on both? So ponder it. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And whoever sends an animal and is its driver, then what it injures in its initial rush he is liable), meaning if a person sends an animal and drives it, then everything it injures in its initial rush he is liable for it. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And if he sends a bird or a dog and is not a driver, or his animal escapes and it injures property or a person by night or day, he is not liable), meaning in all these forms. As for the bird, because its body does not bear driving, so the presence and absence of driving are equal, so he is not liable absolutely, unlike the animal, because its body bears driving, so driving is considered in it. Hence they said: If he sends a falcon in the sanctuary and it kills, the sender is not liable. As for the dog, because even though it can bear driving, however, driving has not actually occurred from him by walking behind it, nor constructively by injuring in the initial rush of the sending, and transgression is by driving, so he is not liable. This is because the principle is that a voluntary act is attributed to its doer, and it is not permissible to attribute it to another. For we abandoned that in the case of an animal when driving occurred from it, so we attributed it to him by istihsan to protect lives and property. And when driving does not occur from it, it remains on the principle, and it is not permissible to attribute it to him due to the absence of the act from him directly or by causation. This is unlike when he sends a dog for hunting, where what it injures is eaten even if he is not its driver actually or constructively, because the need for hunting with it necessitates attribution to the sender as long as the dog remains in that direction and does not turn away, since there is no other way to hunt. This is because hunting with it is legislated, and if driving were required, the door would be closed, while it is open, so it is attributed to him. And if [the prey] disappears from his sight with the prey, and there is no need for him in terms of liability for aggression, then it remains on the principle, so it is attributed to the dog, because it is free in its act, and the sender is not suitable, so its act is not attributed to another.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling regarding liability for injuries caused by animals. It begins by stating the core rule: if a person sends an animal and acts as its driver (sa'iq), he is liable for any injury the animal causes in its initial rush (fi fawriha). If he sends a bird or a dog without driving it, or if his animal escapes and causes injury, he is not liable. The commentator explains that the bird's body cannot be driven, so driving is irrelevant; the animal's body can be driven, so driving matters. For the dog, even though it can be driven, actual driving (walking behind) or constructive driving (injury in the initial rush) is absent, so no liability. The text then discusses various scenarios: tying an animal to a moving caravan (where the tier is liable because he implicitly commands the leader), the case of a rider with a rear rider, driver, and leader (diyah divided in quarters), stumbling over an obstacle (liability on the one who placed it), and prodding (nakhs) where the rider and prodder share liability. The commentator notes the apparent inconsistency in attributing liability to both the direct agent (rider) and the cause (prodder) when the general rule gives priority to the direct agent, and invites reflection.

Key Terms
فور (fawr): The immediate rush or initial moment after sending; an injury occurring in that state is attributed to the sender.
سائق (sa'iq): A person who drives an animal from behind, as opposed to a leader (qa'id) who leads from front. In this text, it includes both actual and constructive driving.
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference that overrides analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more suitable ruling. Here, it allows attributing the animal's act to the driver even though the animal has volition, to protect lives and property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a person sends a dog without driving it and the dog attacks a person, is the sender liable?
A) Yes, because the dog is under his control.
B) Yes, but only if the dog is trained.
C) No, because the dog's act is voluntary and not attributed to the sender without driving.
D) No, but the sender must pay compensation out of mercy.

Key Takeaway
Driving is the key condition for liability in animal injuries by Hanafi law; without it, the animal's act is its own responsibility.

Multiple Parties in Animal Injuries: Tying, Riding, and Prodding
Further commentary: multiple agent scenarios in animal liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine liability when multiple parties are involved in animal-related injuries (tying to caravan, rider with driver/leader, prodding).

Core Idea
When multiple actors contribute to an injury, liability is apportioned based on direct and indirect causation, with the direct agent generally primary.

The commentary extends the discussion to scenarios where several parties participate in an animal's movement or control. A camel tied to a moving caravan, a rider with a driver and leader, and a prodding case all illustrate how the Hanafi school assigns liability by analyzing direct agency (mubasharah) and causation (tasabbub). This lesson guides you through these cases and the subtle exception in the prodding scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios and the parties
There are three main scenarios: (1) tying a camel to a moving/stationary caravan, (2) rider with rear rider, driver, and leader, (3) a person prodding a mounted animal. Identify all human actors in each.
Note the distinction between moving and stationary caravan for the tie case.
2
Apply the principle of direct causation vs. causation
Generally, the direct agent (mubashir) is primarily liable over the indirect cause (sabab). See how this plays out in each scenario.
The rider is a direct agent; the one who placed an obstacle is a cause.
3
Analyze the caravan tie cases
When a camel is tied to a moving caravan, the tier is liable (he implicitly commands the leader). When stationary, the leader is liable. Recourse depends on knowledge.
Knowledge of the tier changes recourse: if the leader knew, no recourse; if ignorant, recourse.
4
Understand the rider, driver, leader scenario
When a rider, rear rider, driver, and leader are all present, the diyah is divided into quarters among them. The rider and rear rider also pay kaffarah.
Each is considered a cause of the injury.
5
Examine the prodding exception
When a third party prods an animal and it tramples someone, both the rider and the prodder are liable if the injury occurs in the initial rush. This seems to contradict the priority of direct agent, but the commentator explains it is because the death results from the combined weight of rider and animal plus the prodding.
The commentator invites reflection on why the general rule is set aside here.
