كتاب الديات باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها وغير ذلك
الرابط ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان سائقها فما أصابت في فورها ضمن وإن أرسل طيراً أو كلباً ولم يكن سائقاً أو انفلتت دابته فأصابت مالاً أو آدمياً ليلاً أو نهاراً لا يضمن وفي فقء به يرجع بعير آخر ربط والقطار يسير لأن الرابط أمر بالقود دلالة وإذا لم يعلم لا يمكنه التحفظ عنه ولكن جهله لا ينفي وجوب الضمان عليه لتحقق الإتلاف منه وإنما ينفي الإثم فيكون قرار الضمان على الرابط، وأما إذا ربط والإبل واقفة ضمنها عاقلة القائد ولا يرجعون على عاقلة الرابط بما لحقهم من الضمان لأن القائد رضي بذلك والتلف قد اتصل بفعله فلا وهو القياس فيما إذا لم يعلم لأن الجهل لا ينافي التسبب ولا الضمان إلا أنا استحسنا الرجوع لما ذكرنا وفي الجامع الصغير : رجل قاد قطاراً في طريق المسلمين فجاء وربطه والقائد لا يعلم به أو علم فأصاب ذلك البعير إنساناً فضمانه على القائد دون الرابط وإن كان كل واحد منهما متسبباً للإتلاف وهل يرجع على عاقلة الرابط؟ إن علم لا يرجع، وإن لم يعلم يرجع ولم يفصل محمد رحمه الله في الجامع الصغير بين ما إذا ربط البعير بالقطار والقطار يسير وفي بعض كتب النوادر : وإن كان القطار لا حالة الربط يسير فقادها القائد بعد الربط لا يرجع القائد على عاقلة الرابط، علم القائد بربطه أو لم يعلم. وإن حالة الربط فالقائد يرجع على عاقلة الرابط إذا لم يعلم بربطه . وفي المنتقى : وإذا سار الرجل على دابته وخلفه رديف وخلف الدابة سائق وأمامها قائد فوطئت إنساناً فالدية عليهم أرباعاً وعلى الراكب والرديف الكفارة، وإذا سار الرجل على دابته في الطريق فعثرت بحجر وضعه رجل أو قد كان بناه رجل أو بماء قد صبه رجل فوقعت على إنسان وأتلفته فالضمان على الذي وضع الحجر في المكان أو الماء لأنه مسبب في هذا الإتلاف
وهو متعد في هذا السبب ولا ضمان على الراكب قالوا ولو نخس الدابة رجل فوطئت إنساناً فالضمان عليهما إن وطئت في فور النخس لأن الموت حصل بثقل الراكب وفعل الناخس فيكون مضافاً إليهما أقول : ولقائل أن يقول الراكب مباشر فيما أتلفت بالوطء لحصول التلف بثقله وثقل الدابة جميعاً كما صرحوا به والناخس مسبب كما مر في الكتاب ، وإذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب فالإضافة إلى المباشر أولى كما صرحوا به لا سيما في مسألة الراكب والسائق فما بالهم صرحوا هنا بإضافة الفعل إلى الراكب والناخس معاً وحكموا بوجوب الدية عليهما جميعاً فتدبره قال رحمه الله: (ومن أرسل بهيمة وكان سائقها فما أصابت في فورها ضمن يعني إذا أرسل إنسان بهيمة وساقها فكل شيء أصابته في فورها فإنه يضمنه .
قال رحمه الله : (وإن أرسل طيراً أو كلباً ولم يكن سائقاً أو انفلتت دابته فأصابت مالاً أو آدمياً ليلاً أو نهاراً لا يضمن أي في هذه الصور كلها ؛ أما الطير فلأن بدنه لا يحتمل السوق فصار وجود السوق وعدمه سواء فلا يضمن مطلقاً بخلاف الدابة فإن بدنها يحمل السوق
فيعتبر فيها السوق ومن ثم قالوا ولو أرسل بازياً في الحرم فقتل لا يضمن المرسل، وأما الكلب فلأنه وإن كان يحتمل السوق لكنه لم يوجد منه السوق حقيقة بأن يمشي خلفه ولا حكماً بأن يصيب على فور الإرسال والتعدي يكون بالسوق فلا يضمن، وهذا لأن الأصل أن الفعل الاختياري يضاف إلى فعل صاحبه ولا يجوز إضافته إلى غيره لأنا تركنا ذلك في فعل البهمية إذا وجد منه السوق فأضفناه إليه استحساناً صيانة للأنفس والأموال، وإذا لم يوجد منه السوق بقي على الأصل ولا يجوز إضافته إليه لعدم الفعل منه مباشرة وتسبباً بخلاف ما إذا أرسل الكلب على صيد حيث يؤكل ما أصابه وإن لم يكن سائقاً له حقيقة ولا حكماً لأن الحاجة مست إلى الاصطياد به فأضيف إلى المرسل ما دام الكلب في تلك الجهة ولم يفتر عنها إذ لا طريق للاصطياد سواه، وهذا لأن الاصطياد به مشروع ولو شرط السوق لاستد بابه وهو مفتوح فأضيف إليه، ولو غاب عن بصره مع الصيد ولا حاجة إليه في حق ضمان العدوان فبقي على الأصل فكان مضافاً إلى الكلب لأنه مختار في فعله ولا يصلح المرسل فلا يضاف فعله إلى غيره.
The book of diyat, chapter of injury by an animal and injury upon it and other things. The one who ties: And whoever sends an animal and is its driver, then what it injures in its initial rush he is liable. And if he sends a bird or a dog and is not a driver, or his animal escapes and it injures property or a person by night or day, he is not liable. And in [the case of] a camel tied to a moving caravan, because the tier implicitly commands the leading, and if he does not know, he cannot guard against it, but his ignorance does not negate the obligation of liability upon him due to the actual destruction from him, but it negates sin, so the liability rests on the tier. But if he ties while the camels are standing, the clan of the leader is liable, and they do not seek recourse from the clan of the tier for the liability that befell them because the leader consented to that and the destruction was connected to his act. That is the analogy when he does not know, because ignorance does not preclude causation nor liability, but we practiced istihsan for recourse as we mentioned. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: A man led a caravan in the path of the Muslims, then someone came and tied [an animal to it] while the leader did not know or knew, and that camel injured a person, so the liability is on the leader, not the tier, though each is a cause of the destruction. And does he have recourse against the clan of the tier? If he knew, no recourse; if he did not know, he has recourse. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) did not distinguish in al-Jami' al-Saghir between whether the camel was tied while the caravan was moving. And in some books of al-Nawadir: If the caravan was not moving at the time of tying, and the leader led after the tying, the leader has no recourse against the clan of the tier, whether he knew of the tying or not. And if the caravan was moving, the leader has recourse against the clan of the tier if he did not know of the tying. And in al-Muntaqa: If a man rides his animal and behind him is a rear rider, and behind the animal is a driver, and in front of it is a leader, and it tramples a person, the blood money is upon them in quarters, and upon the rider and the rear rider is the expiation. And if a man rides his animal in the road and it stumbles over a stone placed by a man or a wall built by a man or water poured by a man, and it falls on a person and destroys him, the liability is upon the one who placed the stone or the water, because he is a cause in this destruction, and he is transgressing in this cause, and there is no liability on the rider. They said: And if a man prods an animal and it tramples a person, the liability is on both if the trampling occurred in the initial rush of the prodding, because death occurred by the weight of the rider and the act of the prodder, so it is attributed to both. I say: One could say the rider is a direct agent in what is destroyed by trampling because the destruction occurs by his weight and the animal's weight together, as they stated, and the prodder is a cause, as previously in the book. And when direct agent and cause are combined, attribution to the direct agent is preferable, as they stated, especially in the case of the rider and the driver. So why did they state here to attribute the act to both the rider and the prodder and rule that the blood money is obligatory on both? So ponder it. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And whoever sends an animal and is its driver, then what it injures in its initial rush he is liable), meaning if a person sends an animal and drives it, then everything it injures in its initial rush he is liable for it. He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And if he sends a bird or a dog and is not a driver, or his animal escapes and it injures property or a person by night or day, he is not liable), meaning in all these forms. As for the bird, because its body does not bear driving, so the presence and absence of driving are equal, so he is not liable absolutely, unlike the animal, because its body bears driving, so driving is considered in it. Hence they said: If he sends a falcon in the sanctuary and it kills, the sender is not liable. As for the dog, because even though it can bear driving, however, driving has not actually occurred from him by walking behind it, nor constructively by injuring in the initial rush of the sending, and transgression is by driving, so he is not liable. This is because the principle is that a voluntary act is attributed to its doer, and it is not permissible to attribute it to another. For we abandoned that in the case of an animal when driving occurred from it, so we attributed it to him by istihsan to protect lives and property. And when driving does not occur from it, it remains on the principle, and it is not permissible to attribute it to him due to the absence of the act from him directly or by causation. This is unlike when he sends a dog for hunting, where what it injures is eaten even if he is not its driver actually or constructively, because the need for hunting with it necessitates attribution to the sender as long as the dog remains in that direction and does not turn away, since there is no other way to hunt. This is because hunting with it is legislated, and if driving were required, the door would be closed, while it is open, so it is attributed to him. And if [the prey] disappears from his sight with the prey, and there is no need for him in terms of liability for aggression, then it remains on the principle, so it is attributed to the dog, because it is free in its act, and the sender is not suitable, so its act is not attributed to another.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn ruling regarding liability for injuries caused by animals. It begins by stating the core rule: if a person sends an animal and acts as its driver (sa'iq), he is liable for any injury the animal causes in its initial rush (fi fawriha). If he sends a bird or a dog without driving it, or if his animal escapes and causes injury, he is not liable. The commentator explains that the bird's body cannot be driven, so driving is irrelevant; the animal's body can be driven, so driving matters. For the dog, even though it can be driven, actual driving (walking behind) or constructive driving (injury in the initial rush) is absent, so no liability. The text then discusses various scenarios: tying an animal to a moving caravan (where the tier is liable because he implicitly commands the leader), the case of a rider with a rear rider, driver, and leader (diyah divided in quarters), stumbling over an obstacle (liability on the one who placed it), and prodding (nakhs) where the rider and prodder share liability. The commentator notes the apparent inconsistency in attributing liability to both the direct agent (rider) and the cause (prodder) when the general rule gives priority to the direct agent, and invites reflection.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubashir): Direct agent who physically causes the injury.
مسبب (musabbib): Indirect cause who sets the stage for the injury but does not physically commit it.
نخس (nakhs): Prodding or poking an animal to spur it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a rider, driver, and leader are all present and the animal tramples someone, how is the blood money divided?
A) Only the rider pays.
B) The rider and driver pay half each.
C) It is divided among all four (rider, rear rider, driver, leader).
D) The leader pays because he guides the animal.

Key Takeaway
Liability for animal injuries can be shared or transferred depending on causation and knowledge of the parties.

The Condition of Driving in Dog Liability: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the condition of driving for dog liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on dog liability and identify the condition of driving that determines liability according to the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The sender of a dog is liable only if he drives or leads it — driving is the decisive condition for attribution of harm.

In this passage, the commentator explores a key condition for liability when a dog causes harm: the sender must be driving or leading the animal. You will encounter an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf, who holds the sender liable regardless, and Muhammad (along with Abu Hanifa), who insists on driving or leading as a necessary condition. By understanding this dispute, you will grasp how the Hanafi school refines the concept of causation in tort law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Issue
The passage focuses on whether the mere act of sending a dog (without driving or leading) makes the sender liable for injuries caused by the dog. Pinpoint the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
The text also discusses related distinctions: trained vs. untrained dogs, sending for hunting vs. on the road, and the effect of turning or stopping.
2
State the Two Positions
Clearly state Abu Yusuf's position (absolute liability) and Muhammad's position (liability only if driving or leading). Note that Abu Hanifa agrees with Muhammad.
Abu Yusuf's view is mentioned at the end of the passage.
3
Analyze the Dalil for Each Position
Abu Yusuf analogizes with sending an animal (bahima) on the road: the sender is liable even without driving. Muhammad responds that a dog has its own volition, unlike a beast, and the sender's control is only effective through driving or leading. Hunting is an exception due to necessity.
The text also mentions the distinction between trained (mu'allam) and untrained dogs, which affects liability when the dog deviates.
4
Determine the Tarjih and Mufta Bihi
The text reports that al-Tahawi and Abu al-Layth gave fatwa on Muhammad's position, making it the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view. Conclude that in Hanafi fatwa, driving or leading is required for liability.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; both views are valid but the latter is preferred.

Source Text

وقوله سائقاً» قيد في الكلب دون الطير، وقيد في الدابة بالانفلات لأنه لو أرسلها يضمن وفي المبسوط : إذا أرسل دابة في طريق المسلمين فما أصابت في فورها فالمرسل ضمن لأن سيرها مضاف إليه ما دامت تسير على سننها، ولو انعطفت عنه يمنة أو يسرة انقطع حكم الإرسال إلا إذا لم يكن له طريق آخر سواه، وكذا إذا وقفت ثم سارت أي ينقطع حكم الإرسال بالوقفة أيضاً كما ينقطع بالعطفة بخلاف ما إذا وقف الكلب بعد الإرسال في الاصطياد ثم سار فأخذ الصيد لأن تلك الوقفة تحقق مقصود المرسل لتمكنه من الصيد وهذه تنافي مقصود المرسل لأن مقصوده السير فينقطع به حكم الإرسال وبخلاف ما إذا أرسله إلى صيد فأصاب نفساً أو مالاً في فوره حيث لا يضمن من أرسله، وفي إرسال البهيمة في الطريق يضمن لأنه شغل الطريق تعدياً فيضمن ما تولد منه، وأما الإرسال للاصطياد فمباح ولا ينسب بوصف التعدي؛ كذا ذكره في النهاية. وظاهره سواء كان سائقاً لها أو لا . وذكر قاضيخان ولو أن رجلاً أرسل بهيمة وكان سائقاً لها ضمن ما أصابت في فورها وكذا لو أرسل كلبه وكان سائقاً له يضمن ما أتلف، ولو لم يكن سائقاً لا يضمن. وكذا لو أشلى كلبه على رجل فعقره أو مزق ثيابه لا يضمن إلا أن يسوقه وقيل إذا أرسل كلبه وهو لا يمشي خلفه فعقر إنساناً أو أتلف غيره إن لم يكن معلماً لا غير المعلم يذهب بطبع نفسه وإن كان معلماً ضمن إن مر على الوجه الذي أرسله لأنه ذهب بإرسال صاحبه أما إذا أخذ يمنة أو يسرة فلا يضمن لأنه مال عن سنن الإرسال إلا إذا كان خلفه. ولو أشلي كلبه حتى عض رجلاً لا يضمن كما لو أرسل بازياً، وعن أبي يوسف يضمن سواء كان يسوقه أو يقوده أو لا يقوده ولا يسوقه كما لو أرسل البهيمة. وعند محمد أنه إن كان سائقاً أو قائداً يضمن وإلا فلا وبه أخذ الطحاوي والفقيه أبو الليث كان يفتي يضمن
His statement 'driver' is a restriction concerning the dog but not the bird, and a restriction concerning the animal by escape because if he sent it he is liable. And in al-Mabsut: If he sends an animal in the road of the Muslims, what it injures in its initial rush the sender is liable, because its movement is attributed to him as long as it goes straight, and if it turns right or left, the rule of sending is severed unless there is no other road. Similarly, if it stops and then goes, the rule of sending is severed by the stop as by the turn, unlike if the dog stops after sending in hunting and then goes and catches prey, because that stop fulfills the sender's purpose of enabling the hunt, while this stop contradicts the sender's purpose because his purpose is movement, so the rule of sending is severed. And unlike if he sends it to hunt and it injures a person or property in its initial rush, where the sender is not liable. And sending an animal in the road makes him liable because he occupied the road wrongfully, so he is liable for what results from it. As for sending for hunting, it is permissible and not described as transgression; thus it is mentioned in al-Nihaya. Its apparent meaning is the same whether he is its driver or not. And Qadikhan mentioned: If a man sends an animal and is its driver, he is liable for what it injures in its initial rush; likewise if he sends his dog and is its driver, he is liable for what it destroys, and if not a driver, he is not liable. Similarly, if he sets his dog on a man and it wounds him or tears his clothes, he is not liable unless he drives it. And it is said: If he sends his dog while not walking behind it and it wounds a person or destroys something else, if it is not trained, the untrained one goes by its own nature; if it is trained, he is liable if it goes on the path he sent it, because it went by the sending of its owner, but if it goes right or left, he is not liable because it deviates from the course of sending unless it is behind him. And if he sets his dog until it bites a man, he is not liable as if he sent a falcon. And from Abu Yusuf: he is liable whether he drives it, leads it, or does not lead or drive it, as if he sent an animal. And according to Muhammad, if he is a driver or leader, he is liable; otherwise not. And al-Tahawi adopted this, and the jurist Abu al-Layth gave fatwa that he is liable.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, focusing on the condition of 'driving' (sa'iq) and elaborating on the liability for sending a dog. It explains that the word 'driver' in the matn is a restriction specifically for the dog but not for the bird, and for the animal it is restricted by 'escape' (i.e., if it escapes, no liability). The text then cites al-Mabsut: if an animal is sent in the road, the sender is liable as long as it goes straight; if it turns or stops, the rule of sending is severed, except for a dog in hunting where a stop may fulfill the purpose. Sending an animal in the road is transgression because it occupies the road wrongfully, so the sender is liable for any resulting harm. However, sending for hunting is permissible and not transgression, so liability differs. Qadikhan's report says: if a man sends an animal and drives it, he is liable; same for a dog if driven. If not driven, no liability. Setting a dog on a person without driving does not make the sender liable, unless he drives it. If the dog is trained and goes in the direction sent, the sender is liable; if it deviates, not. The intra-Hanafi dispute is then presented: Abu Yusuf holds the sender liable regardless of driving or leading, drawing analogy with sending an animal. Muhammad holds that liability only attaches if the sender is driving or leading the dog. The text notes that al-Tahawi and Abu al-Layth followed Muhammad's view, which is the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Key Terms
قيد (qayd): A restriction or condition that limits the scope of a ruling. Here, the word 'driver' in the matn is a restriction that limits liability to cases where driving exists.
قائد (qa'id): A leader who leads the animal from the front. In the context of dog liability, Muhammad includes both driving (from behind) and leading (from front) as conditions for liability.
معلّم (mu'allam): A trained dog that responds to its owner's commands. A trained dog that goes in the direction sent creates liability for the sender, unlike an untrained dog that acts on its own nature.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Abu Hanifa's position: the sender is never liable.
C) Muhammad's position: liability only if the sender is driving or leading.
D) All three agree; there is no dispute.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for sending a dog requires that the sender be driving or leading it; the mere act of sending without control does not make him liable (mufta bihi view).

Compensation for Animal Eye Injury: Sheep vs. Beast of Burden
States the ruling for injury to a sheep's eye and then for a beast of burden's eye, with a Shafi'i dissent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for compensation of a beast of burden's eye, identify the Shafi'i counter-argument, and explain why the Hanafi school relies on the hadith over qiyas.

Core Idea
A beast of burden's eye is worth a quarter of its value; a sheep's eye is only its market loss.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a split between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding compensation for injuring the eye of certain animals. While for a sheep only the decrease in market value is paid, for a beast of burden (horse, donkey) the Hanafis prescribe a fixed quarter. The dispute hinges on whether to follow a specific hadith or analogize to sheep. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to evaluate textual versus analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two categories: sheep (only decrease in value) and beasts of burden (fixed quarter). Note the animals included: donkey, horse, and even a slaughter animal (badana) due to multiple uses.
The text explicitly says 'ربع القيمة' for the latter.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all animal eyes should be compensated by decrease in value only, analogizing to sheep.
His proof is qiyas: sheep serve a single purpose (meat), so beasts should follow.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i analogy: the eye of an animal, like a sheep's, is not a limb with independent value; it only affects the animal's market price.
This assumes no fixed shar'i measure exists.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafis cite a hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled a quarter for a beast's eye. They also argue that beasts have multiple purposes (riding, work, beauty) making them resemble humans, who have fixed diya for limbs.
The hadith cannot be overridden by qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: for a horse worth 4000, Hanafi: pay 1000; Shafi'i: pay only the market decrease (say 500).
This affects actual financial liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عين شاة لقصاب ضمن النقصان وفي عين بدنة الجزار والحمار والفرس ربع القيمة . قال رحمه الله : (وفي فقء عين شاة لقصاب ضمن النقصان) لأن المقصود من اللحم فلا يعتبر فيها إلا النقصان. قال رحمه الله : (وفي عين بدنة الجزار والحمار والفرس ربع القيمة) وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : ليس فيه إلا النقصان أيضاً اعتباراً بالشاة. ولنا ما عليه الصلاة والسلام قضى في عين الدابة بربع القيمة قال في العناية : فإن قيل يجوز أن يكون قضاء رسول الله عليه الصلاة والسلام فيما يؤكل، فالجواب أن الشيء الذي أوجب في غير المأكول من اللحم والركوب والزينة والجمال والعمل موجود في مأكول اللحم فيلحق به اهـ. ولأن فيها مقاصد سوى اللحم كالركوب وللزينة واللحم والعمل فمن هذا الوجه يشبه الآدمي وقد تمسك بغيره كالأكل ومن هذا الوجه يشبه المأكولات فعملنا ذلك
The eye of a sheep (for a butcher): he guarantees the decrease in value. And the eye of a beast of burden for the butcher, and the donkey, and the horse: a quarter of the value. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And in the gouging out of the eye of a sheep for a butcher: he guarantees the decrease in value) because the purpose is meat, so only the decrease is considered. The author (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And in the eye of a beast of burden for the butcher, and the donkey, and the horse: a quarter of the value.) Al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: There is only the decrease in value therein as well, by analogy with the sheep. Our proof: the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) ruled in the eye of a beast (dābba) as a quarter of its value. Al-'Inaya says: If it is said: Perhaps the ruling of the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) was concerning an animal that is eaten, then the answer: The thing for which he obligated (the quarter) in non-edible animals—from meat, riding, ornament, beauty, and work—exists in edible meat animals, so they are attached to it. End quote. And because they (beasts of burden) have purposes other than meat, such as riding, ornament, meat, and work; from this angle they resemble a human, and they have another aspect like edible animals; so we acted accordingly.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the compensation (daman) for the loss of an eye of certain animals. The ruling distinguishes between a sheep and a beast of burden (like a donkey or horse). For a sheep, the compensation is only the decrease in value (nuqsan) because its primary purpose is meat; thus, the eye itself is not independently valued beyond its effect on the carcass price. For a beast of burden (including a donkey, horse, or a cow used for meat? Actually the text says 'بدنة الجزار' which might be a slaughter animal, but then includes الحمار والفرس - donkey and horse, so they are beasts of burden), the compensation is a fixed quarter of the animal's value. The author then mentions al-Shafi'i's objection: he argued that all animals should be treated like sheep, with only the decrease in value due to the defect. The Hanafi response provides two proofs: first, a specific hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled that the compensation for a beast's eye is a quarter of its value. Second, that beasts of burden have multiple purposes (riding, work, etc.) beyond just meat, so they are more akin to humans (where the eye has a fixed diya) in some respects, and therefore cannot be treated as mere meat animals. The passage also addresses a potential objection: the hadith might apply only to edible beasts (like cows). But the Hanafis counter that the rationale (the multiple benefits) applies to both edible and non-edible beasts, so the ruling extends to all beasts of burden. The key point is that the basis for compensation is not pure market value but the purpose and utility of the animal.

Key Terms
النقصان (al-nuqṣān): The decrease in market value of an animal due to the loss of its eye, i.e., the difference between its sound value and its value after the injury.
البدنة (al-badana): A large sacrificial animal, typically a camel or cow. In this context, it may refer to a beast of burden or an animal meant for slaughter, but the ruling is linked to the animal's utility.
ربع القيمة (rub' al-qīma): One quarter of the animal's total market value at the time of injury, used as the fixed compensation for the eye of a beast of burden.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the compensation for gouging out the eye of a horse?
A) Only the decrease in the horse's market value.
B) One quarter of the horse's value.
C) Half of the horse's value.
D) The full price of the horse.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, compensation for a beast of burden's eye is a fixed quarter of its value, not merely the market decrease, based on a specific prophetic hadith.

Compensation for Eye Injuries to Animals: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains Hanafi ruling and proof for compensation in animal eye injuries, with Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on compensation for eye injuries to sheep compared to camels/donkeys/horses, explain the hadith proof, and distinguish the Shafi'i position based on analogy.

Core Idea
Poking out the eye of a horse requires quarter its value — explicit hadith overrides analogy to sheep.

This lesson examines a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on compensating eye injuries to certain animals. While both schools agree on deficiency for sheep, the Hanafis rule quarter value for animals with multiple purposes like riding. The dispute hinges on whether a Prophetic hadith or qiyas governs.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two categories: sheep (deficiency) and camel/donkey/horse (quarter value). Understand the basis: the purpose of the animal.
The ruling is from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, a core Hanafi matn.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Al-Shafi'i argues that all animals should be treated like sheep: only deficiency. This is based on qiyas (analogy) by the common feature of being edible.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i qiyas: all animals are similar in edibility, so the ruling for sheep should apply universally. This is a straightforward analogy.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response uses a Prophetic hadith that specifically judges the eye of a beast (dābbah) at quarter value. Additionally, animals like horses and camels serve purposes beyond meat (riding, work, adornment) which raises their status, similar to a human (full diyah), but reduced because they are also edible.
The hadith overrides qiyas because it is a direct textual proof.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: For a horse worth $1000 intact, $800 after injury, Hanafi requires $250 (quarter), Shafi'i requires $200 (deficiency). This affects financial justice in transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وفي فقء عين شاة لقصاب ضمن النقصان) لأن المقصود من اللحم فلا يعتبر فيها إلا النقصان. قال رحمه الله : (وفي عين بدنة الجزار والحمار والفرس ربع القيمة) وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : ليس فيه إلا النقصان أيضاً اعتباراً بالشاة. ولنا ما عليه الصلاة والسلام قضى في عين الدابة بربع القيمة قال في العناية : فإن قيل يجوز أن يكون قضاء رسول الله عليه الصلاة والسلام فيما يؤكل، فالجواب أن الشيء الذي أوجب في غير المأكول من اللحم والركوب والزينة والجمال والعمل موجود في مأكول اللحم فيلحق به اهـ. ولأن فيها مقاصد سوى اللحم كالركوب وللزينة واللحم والعمل فمن هذا الوجه يشبه الآدمي وقد تمسك بغيره كالأكل ومن هذا الوجه يشبه المأكولات فعملنا ذلك، بأنه روي أن الشاة [؟] . وفي العناية : وإنما قال بدنة ليشمل البقر والإبل فإن الحكم فيها واحد وهو ربع القيمة. وفي العيني على الهداية: وفي فقء عين بدنة الجزار - بفتح الجيم - وهو ما اتخذ للنحر يقع على الذكور والأنثى؛ كذا في الطحاوي والجزر القطع وجزر الجزور نحرها والجزار هو الذي ينحر البقرة اهـ. والله أعلم.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: '(And for poking out the eye of a sheep belonging to a butcher, he guarantees the deficiency) because the purpose is meat, so only the deficiency is considered. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And for poking out the eye of a butcher's camel [badanah], donkey, and horse, one-quarter of the value).' Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: There is only the deficiency also, by analogy with the sheep. Our proof is that the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, judged regarding the eye of an animal as one-quarter of its value. Al-'Inayah said: If it is said that the judgment of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, may be concerning what is eaten, the answer is that the attribute that caused the ruling in the non-edible – meat, riding, adornment, beauty, and work – exists in the edible meat, so it is annexed to it. And because there are purposes besides meat, like riding, adornment, meat, and work, from this aspect it resembles a human, and from another aspect it resembles edible things, so we acted accordingly, as it is narrated that the sheep... Al-'Inayah said: He only said 'badanah' to include cattle and camels, for the ruling is the same for both: one-quarter of the value. Al-'Ayni on Al-Hidayah said: As for poking out the eye of a butcher's badanah – with a fatha on the jim – it is an animal prepared for slaughter, used for both males and females; likewise in Al-Tahawi; al-jazr is cutting, jazr al-jazur is slaughtering it, and al-jazzar is the one who slaughters the cow. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafis and Imam al-Shafi'i on the compensation for eye injuries to animals. The author presents the matn ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq: For a sheep (which is primarily for meat), if its eye is poked out, the injurer must pay the difference in value between a sheep with two eyes and one with one eye – that is, the deficiency. For a camel prepared for slaughter (badanah), a donkey, and a horse, the compensation is fixed at one-quarter of the animal's full value. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that all animals should only have the deficiency payment, by analogy with the sheep. The Hanafi response is twofold. First, a hadith explicitly judges the eye of a beast of burden (dābbah) at one-quarter of its value. If one objects that the hadith might only apply to animals eaten, the answer is that the purpose (of the animal) includes riding, beauty, and work – attributes also found in edible animals, so the ruling extends. Second, these animals (horse, donkey, camel) serve multiple purposes beyond meat, such as riding and labour, which makes them akin to a human in value (whose eye would require full diyah), but since they are also edible, the compensation is reduced to one-quarter. The term 'badanah' is explained as covering both cattle and camels, and it refers to an animal kept for sacrifice or slaughter.

Key Terms
بدنة (badanah): A camel or cow prepared for sacrifice or slaughter, used here for any large sacrificial animal.
جبار (jubbār): In the hadith 'al-'ajmā' jubbār', it means that injury caused by an animal without direct human action is not compensable (hader).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the compensation for poking out the eye of a camel (badanah) that belongs to a butcher?
A) Only the deficiency in value
B) Quarter of the full value
C) Full value of the camel
D) Half of the value

Key Takeaway
For animals with multiple purposes like riding and work, the Hanafi school prescribes quarter compensation based on a Prophetic hadith, overriding the analogy to sheep.

Slave Injury Liability: The Single Payment Rule
Explains ordering of chapters and states the basic ruling that a slave's injury incurs only one payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a slave's injury and explain why compensation is limited to a single payment.

Core Idea
A slave's injury liability is limited to one payment: either the slave himself or his value, never both.

This lesson introduces a fundamental rule in Hanafi fiqh regarding injuries caused by slaves. We will explore why the chapter on slave injuries is placed after the chapter on animal injuries, and then examine the core ruling: the victim is entitled to only one form of compensation. This rule highlights the unique legal status of slaves as property with agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on slave injury liability
Read the matn statement: 'جناية المملوك لا توجب إلا دفعاً واحداً لو محلاً لها وإلا قيمة واحدة'. Determine the precise obligation on the owner.
2
Understand 'دفع واحد'
Analyze the term 'one payment'. What are the two possible forms? When does each apply?
3
Examine the chapter ordering rationale
Review the author's critique of previous explanations for placing the slave chapter after the animal chapter. Identify the flaw in al-ʿInāyah's reasoning.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Consider a hypothetical: if a slave injures a free person, and the slave's value is 100 dinars but the injury demands 200 dinars, what must the owner pay? Explain using the single payment rule.

باب جناية المملوك والجناية عليه لما فرغ رحمه من بيان حكم جناية المالك وهو الحر والجناية عليه شرع في بيان أحكام جناية المملوك وهو العبد، وأخره لانحطاط رتبه العبد عن رتبه الحر؛ كذا في الشروح. أقول : فيه شيء وهو أن لقائل أن يقول : لما وقع الفراغ من بيان أحكام جناية الحر على الحر مطلقاً بقي منه بيان حكم جناية الحر على العبد فالأظهر أن يقال : لما فرغ من بيان جناية الحر على الحر شرع في بيان جناية المملوك والجناية عليه ولما كان فيه تعلق الملك بالمملوك ألبتة من جانب أخره لانحطاط رتبة المملوك عن المالك قال صاحب العناية : لا يقال العبد لا يكون أدنى منزلة من البهيمة فكيف أخر باب جنايته عن باب جناية البهيمة لأن جناية البهيمة كانت باعتبار الراكب والسائق أو القائد وهم ملاك اهـ. أقول : فيه أيضاً شيء إذ لقائل أن يقول : إن أراد جناية البهيمة كانت باعتبار الراكب أو السائق أو القائد فهو ممنوع فإن جنايتها بطريق النفحة برجلها أو ذنبها وهي تسير لا يكون باعتبار أحد وإلا لوجب عليهم الضمان في تلك الصورة وليس كذلك كما عرف في بابها، وكذا الحال فيما إذا أصابت بيدها أو رجلها حصاة أو نواة أو أثارت غباراً أو حجراً صغيراً فقأ عين إنسان أو أفسد ثوبه، وكذا إذا انفلتت فأصابت مالاً أو آدمياً ليلاً أو نهاراً كما عرف كل ذلك أيضاً في بابها. وإن أراد أن جنايتها قد تكون باعتبار أحد منهم فهو مسلم ولكن لا يتم به تمام التعريف. ويمكن أن يقال : الصور التي لا يجب فيها من فعل البهيمة ضمان على أحد بل يكون فعلها هدراً مما لا يترتب عليه حكم من أحكام الجناية في الشرع. وإنما ذكرت في بابها استطراداً وبناء الكلام هنا على ماله حكم من الأحكام الشرعية فيتم التعريف . قال (جناية المملوك لا توجب إلا دفعاً واحداً لو محلاً لها وإلا قيمة واحدة) أي جناية العبد لا
Chapter on Injury by a Slave and Injury to a Slave. When the author (may Allah have mercy on him) finished explaining the rulings of injury by a free person (owner) and injury to him, he began explaining the rulings of injury by a slave (abd), and he delayed it because the rank of a slave is lower than that of a free person; thus in the commentaries. I say: There is something in this: one could say that since the completion of the exposition of the rulings of injury by a free person upon a free person in general, the ruling of injury by a free person upon a slave remains. So it would be more apparent to say: When he finished the exposition of injury by a free person upon a free person, he began the exposition of injury by a slave and injury to him, and because there is total ownership attachment to the slave from the owner's side, he delayed it due to the lower rank of the slave compared to the owner. The author of al-ʿInāyah said: It cannot be said that a slave is not of a lower status than an animal, so why did he place the chapter on injury by a slave after the chapter on injury by an animal? Because injury by an animal is only considered in relation to the rider, driver, or leader, and they are owners. I say: There is also something in this: one could object: if he means that injury by an animal is only considered in relation to the rider, driver, or leader, that is not acceptable, because its injury by kicking with its leg or tail while walking is not attributed to anyone, otherwise they would be liable in that case, which is not so as known in its chapter. Similarly, if it hits with its hand or foot a pebble, a stone, or raises dust or a small stone that pokes out a person's eye or spoils his garment, and also if it runs away and strikes property or a person by night or day, as all that is known in its chapter. If he means that its injury may be attributed to someone, that is accepted but the definition is not perfect. It can be said: The cases in which no liability arises from the animal's act and its act is considered null (hader) are those that do not result in any ruling of injury in the Shariah. They were mentioned in its chapter as a digression, and the discussion here is based on what has a Shariah ruling, thus the definition is complete. He said: 'Injury by a slave does not necessitate except one payment if there is a place for it, otherwise one value.' That is, injury by a slave does not...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage serves as an introduction to the chapter on injuries involving slaves. The author first explains why the chapter on slaves comes after the chapter on animals: because a slave's rank is lower than a free person but higher than an animal? Actually, there is an objection: why is the slave chapter after the animal chapter when slaves are more valuable? The answer given by al-ʿInāyah is that injuries by animals are only relevant when attributed to a rider/driver (who may be liable), while injuries by animals without attribution are not subject to liability. The author then critiques this reasoning, noting that many animal injuries are not attributed to anyone, yet they are still discussed in the animal chapter as a digression. The key point is that the current chapter deals with injuries that have Shariah rulings (i.e., liability). Finally, the base matn ruling is given: 'Injury by a slave does not necessitate except one payment if there is a place for it, otherwise one value.' That is, if a slave injures someone, the victim is entitled to either the slave himself (if the slave is the object of payment) or a single value if the slave is no longer available or cannot be handed over. This is the fundamental rule for slave injuries.

Key Terms
جناية المملوك (jināyat al-mamlūk): The act of a slave that causes harm to another's life, limb, or property, resulting in liability.
دفع واحد (daf' wāhid): A single payment; either the slave himself (his person) or his value, not both.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because injuries by animals are only considered when attributed to a rider or driver, and such cases are fewer
C) Because the discussion of animal injuries is more important
D) Because the author wanted to end the book with a lighter topic

Key Takeaway
A slave's injury liability is limited to a single payment—either the slave himself or his value—never both.

Liability for Multiple Wounds on a Slave with Changing Status
Complex furu' case on multi-wound diyah with status changes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the arsh and diyah liability for multiple wounds inflicted on a slave whose legal status changes between wounds, applying the Hanafi rule of siraya cutoff.

Core Idea
Status change cuts off siraya: only wounds whose spread is not severed contribute to death liability.

This passage presents a complex furu' case from al-Muhit illustrating how Hanafi jurists apportion diyah when a victim's status changes between multiple wounds. The slave is struck four times, each time after a change in legal status (qinn, mudabbir, mukaateb, free). The key principle is that changes in status (emancipation, kitabah) sever the causal chain (siraya) for earlier wounds, so only wounds whose siraya is intact at the time of death contribute to the death liability. Understanding this case sharpens your ability to handle multi-wound scenarios in jinaaya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of events
List the four wounds in order and note the slave's legal status at the moment each wound was inflicted: first wound (qinn), second wound (mudabbir), third wound (mukaateb), fourth wound (free after kitabah).
The status at the time of the wound determines the arsh amount and the liability for death.
2
Determine arsh for each wound
For each wound, calculate the fixed compensation (arsh) based on the slave's value and status at the time of injury. First wound: half a tenth of full slave value. Second wound: not explicitly given but likely same as first if still slave? Actually the text only details first, third, fourth. For the second wound (mudabbir), the arsh is a fraction of his value as mudabbir. The text omits it for brevity.
The arsh for a mudihah is generally half a tenth of the victim's value (for a slave) or one-twentieth for a free person? Actually for a slave, arsh is based on his value; for a free person, diyah.
3
Apply the siraya cutoff principle
Changes in status (becoming mudabbir, mukaateb, free) cut off the spread (siraya) of prior wounds. The first and second wounds' siraya is cut off by the subsequent status changes, so they contribute nothing to the death. Only the third wound's siraya is considered because it was not cut off before death.
The fourth wound happened when free, so its siraya is also considered, but the text apportions the death one-third to the third wound and two-thirds to the fourth? Actually it says guarantor pays one-third of the value for the third wound's share, and the fourth wound's death liability is already covered by that? This requires careful reading.
4
Apportion death liability among active wounds
Since the death resulted from the combined effect of the third and fourth wounds (the first two having no effect), the death liability is divided between them. The text concludes that the guarantor pays one-third of the slave's value (the arsh for the third wound) as his share of the death, and the fourth wound's death liability is not additional because the slave already had a deficiency from the third wound. The final result: one-third of the slave's value (diminished by four wounds) for the death.
This apportionment uses the principle that each wound's contribution is proportional; the exact fraction (one-third) comes from the fact that the third wound's siraya is considered, and the fourth wound's siraya is already covered by the third's liability due to overlapping effects.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد لرجل شجه رجل موضحة ثم دبره ثم شجه موضحة أخرى ثم كاتبه ثم شجه موضحة ثالثة ثم أدى الكتابة فعتق ثم شجه موضحة رابعة فمات من ذلك فهاهنا حكم الشجاج وحكم النفس، أما حكم الشجاج فالأولى يضمن الشاج نصف عشر قيمته وهو عبد صحيح وأما حكم الشجة الثالثة فإنه يضمن نصف عشر قيمته وهو مدبر مكاتب مشجوج شجتين وأما حكم الشجة الرابعة فإنه يضمن ثلث الدية ولا يضمن الأرش، وأما حكم النفس فلا شيء على الشاج بسراية الشجة الأولى والثانية لأن سرايتهما منقطعة عن الجناية بالعتق والكتابة ويضمن للشجة الثالثة ثلث قيمته وهو مدبر مكاتب مشجوج بأربع شجات ولا يضمن ثلث الدية وإن مات حراً لأن ابتداء الشجة لاقي الكتابة، وإنما يضمن ثلث قيمته لا ربعها لأن الجناية الأولى والثانية حكمهما واحد والشجة الرابعة لاقته وهو حر وموجبها الدية فبان بهذا، واتضح أن النفس إنما تلفت واعتباراً بثلاث جنايات ثلثها بالجناية الأولى وقد هدرت سرايتها وثلثها بالجناية الثالثة وسرايتها معتبرة فيضمن ثلث قيمته مشجوحاً بأربع شجاج لأن ثلاث شجاج منها ضمنها مرة فلا يضمن مرة أخرى، وما تلف بالشجة الرابعة يكون مضموناً على الشاج بالشجة الثالثة لأنه مات وهو منقوص بأربع شجات؛ كذا في المحيط مع اختصار.
A man struck a slave belonging to a man with a head wound, then he made him a mudabbir, then struck him with another head wound, then made him a mukaateb, then struck him with a third head wound, then he paid the kitabah and became free, then struck him with a fourth head wound, and he died from that. Here is the ruling for the wounds and the ruling for the death: As for the ruling for the wounds, for the first, the striker guarantees half of a tenth of his value while he was a sound slave. As for the ruling for the third wound, he guarantees half of a tenth of his value while he was a mudabbir mukaateb with two wounds. As for the ruling for the fourth wound, he guarantees a third of the diyah and does not guarantee the arsh. As for the ruling for the death, nothing is due from the striker for the spread of the first and second wounds because their spread from the injury is cut off by the emancipation and the kitabah. And he guarantees for the third wound a third of his value while he is a mudabbir mukaateb with four wounds, and he does not guarantee a third of the diyah even though he died free, because the beginning of the wound encountered the kitabah. And he only guarantees a third of his value, not a quarter, because the first and second wounds have the same ruling, and the fourth wound encountered him while free, and its requirement is the diyah. So it becomes clear by this. And it is apparent that the death only occurred, and considering three injuries, a third of it was by the first injury but its spread is nullified, and a third of it was by the third injury and its spread is considered, so he guarantees a third of his value wounded with four wounds, because three of the wounds were already guaranteed once, so they are not guaranteed again. And what is lost by the fourth wound is guaranteed against the striker by the third wound, because he died while deficient by four wounds. Thus stated in al-Muhit with abridgment.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed furu' case from al-Muhit demonstrating how Hanafi rules of jinaaya apply when a slave's status changes between multiple intentional wounds (shajj). The scenario: a slave is struck in the head (mudihah) four times. After the first wound, the master makes him a mudabbir (promised freedom after master's death). After the second, he makes him a mukaateb (contract to purchase freedom). After the third, the slave pays his kitabah and becomes free. After the fourth, he dies. The passage distinguishes between the rulings for each wound individually (ahkam al-shijaj) and for the death (hukm al-nafs). For each wound, the guarantor (shajj) must pay the arsh (fixed compensation) based on the slave's value at the time of the wound. For the first wound, the slave was a full slave (qinn), so the arsh is half of a tenth of his undiminished value. For the third wound, the slave was a mudabbir mukaateb with two prior wounds, so the arsh is half of a tenth of his diminished value at that point. For the fourth wound, the slave was free, so the liability is the full diyah on the 'aqila, but here the text stipulates it is one-third of the diyah (because part of the death is attributed to earlier wounds). Regarding the death, the key principle is that the spread (siraya) of the first and second wounds is 'cut off' by the change in status (emancipation and kitabah), meaning no liability for those contributions to death. Only the third wound's spread is considered, so the guarantor pays one-third of the slave's value at the time of the third wound (as a mudabbir mukaateb with four wounds) as his share of the death liability. The fourth wound, though inflicted when free, does not add separate death liability because the death is apportioned across the third and fourth wounds, but the fourth wound's contribution is already covered by the third wound's liability. The passage emphasizes that the total death compensation is one-third of the slave's value, not a quarter, because the first two wounds are treated as one unit. This case illustrates the Hanafi methodology of apportioning diyah across multiple injuries based on the timing and the victim's legal status at each point.

Key Terms
شجة (shajjah): A head wound that reaches the bone, in this context used generally for any head wound.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave promised freedom after his master's death, considered partly free during the master's lifetime.
مكاتب (mukaateb): A slave under a contract of kitabah to purchase freedom, considered partly free.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a slave wounded four times with changing status, why does the striker not guarantee anything for the death from the first two wounds?
A) Because the wounds were minor and did not contribute to death.
B) Because the siraya (spread) of those wounds is cut off by the changes in status (emancipation and kitabah).
C) Because the slave was not yet free at the time of those wounds.
D) Because the first two wounds were accidental, not intentional.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi jinaaya, changes in the victim's legal status after a wound cut off the spread (siraya) of that wound, so only wounds whose siraya remains intact at death contribute to the death liability.

The Master's Option and the Shafi'i Challenge: Slave Jinaaya in Hanafi Fiqh
Inter-madhhab dispute on slave's accidental injury

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a master's liability for his slave's accidental injury, explain the Shafi'i counter-position, and apply the proofs to evaluate the dispute.

Core Idea
For a slave's accidental injury, Hanafi grants the master an option; Shafi'i insists on sale of the slave—the dispute turns on how liability attaches to the slave's person.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentary in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute. We begin with the Hanafi ruling that the master may either surrender the slave or pay ransom. Then we encounter the Shafi'i objection that the slave must be sold. By working through the proofs, you will understand the legal reasoning that divides the schools and the practical consequences for victims and masters.

Guided Path
1
Pinpoint the ruling and its scope
The matn states: for an accidental injury by a slave, the master has an option. Note that 'accidental' is crucial for homicide; for limbs it does not change the rule because qisas is not applicable between slaves or between slaves and freemen for limb injuries.
The restriction 'خطأ' (accidental) is important: for intentional homicide, qisas applies against the slave, so the master does not have the option.
2
State the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the slave's liability attaches to his person (raqaba); he must be sold unless the master voluntarily pays. The victim's right is against the slave's person, not against the master.
This is the key point of disagreement: Hanafi attaches liability to the master via option, Shafi'i attaches it to the slave's person.
3
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, once the master chooses (or is compelled), the matter is closed; the victim cannot pursue the slave after he becomes free. Under Shafi'i, the liability persists on the slave even after freedom, so the victim can claim compensation from the freed slave.
This difference has real-world implications for the slave's future financial liability.
4
Examine the first proof: Quran 2:194
The Shafi'i rely on the general principle of reciprocity: 'So whoever transgresses against you, then transgress against him in the same way as he transgressed against you.' They argue that the slave, as the immediate transgressor, should be liable. The Hanafi response restricts this verse: it is limited by the consideration that the master's right to the slave's life (by ownership) provides an alternative to direct liability on the slave.
The Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to limit the general command.
5
Examine the second proof: Principles of accidental injury
The Hanafi argue that accidental injury should be distanced from the perpetrator (due to his excuse) and attached to the nearest person (the master, who is like an 'aqila). Since the slave has no 'aqila, the liability falls on the master. The Shafi'i counter that a slave's lack of 'aqila does not transfer liability to the master; rather it stays on the slave himself, analogous to a dhimmi who has no 'aqila and is liable personally.
This is a fundamental difference in how each school conceptualizes liability for accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (جنى عبد خطأ دفعه بالجناية فيملكه أو فداه بأرشها) أي إذا جنى العبد خطأ فمولاه بالخيار إن شاء دفعه إلى ولي الجناية فإن دفعه ملكه ولي الجناية، وإن شاء فداه بأرشها . وقوله «خطأ يحترز به من العمد وهذا التقييد إنما يفيد إذا كانت الجناية على النفس لأنها إن كانت عمداً توجب القصاص، وأما إذا كانت على الأطراف لا يفيد التقييد به إذا لا يجري القصاص فيها بين العبيد وبين الأحرار والعبيد. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : جناية العبد تتعلق برقبته يباع فيها إلا أن يقضى المولى الأرش. وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في اتباع الجاني عنده يتبع لا في حالة الرق ولا بعد الحرية والمسألة مختلفة بين الصحابة رضي الله عنهم؛ فعن ابن عباس مثل مذهبنا وعن عمر وعلي مثل مذهبه ؛ له أن الأصل في موجب الجناية أن يجب على الجاني لأنه المتعدي قال الله تعالى فمن اعتدى عليكم فاعتدوا عليه بمثل عليكم ﴾ [البقرة : ١٩٤] إلا أن العاقلة تتحمل عنه ولا عاقلة للعبد فيجب في ذمته كما في الذمي ويتعلق برقبته ويباع فيه كما في الجناية على المال ولنا أن المستحق بالجناية على النفوس نفس الجاني إذا أمكن إلا أن استحقاق النفس قد يكون بطريق الإتلاف عقوبة وقد يكون بطريق التملك والعبد من أهل أن يستحق نفسه بالطريقين فتصير نفسه مستحقة للمجني عليه صيانة عن الهدر إلا أن يختار المولى الفداء فيكون له ذلك لأنه ليس فيه إبطال حق المجني عليه بل مقصود المجني يحصل بذلك بخلاف إتلاف المال فإنه لا يستحق به نفس الجاني أبداً، ولأن الأصل في موجب الجناية خطأ أن يتباعد عن الجاني لكونه معذوراً ولكون الخطأ مرفوعاً شرعاً ويتعلق بأقرب الناس إليه تخفيفاً عن المخطئ وتوقياً عن الإجحاف إلا أن عاقلة العبد ما اعتدى
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: 'A slave committed accidental injury, either he is handed over for the injury and the victim owns him, or he ransoms him with its arsh.' That is: when a slave commits accidental injury, his master has the choice: if he wishes, he hands him over to the guardian of the injury, and then the guardian of the injury owns him; and if he wishes, he ransoms him with its arsh. And his saying 'accidental' avoids intentional injury, and this restriction only benefits when the injury is against the life because if it were intentional it would require retaliation; but if it is against limbs, the restriction does not benefit because retaliation does not apply among slaves and between slaves and freemen. Imam al-Shafi'i said: The injury of a slave attaches to his person (raqaba); he is sold for it unless the master pays the arsh. And the fruit of the difference appears in following the perpetrator: in his school, he is pursued neither in a state of slavery nor after freedom. And the issue is disputed among the Companions: Ibn Abbas held our position; Umar and Ali held his position. His proof is that the original rule in the requirement of injury is that it is required of the perpetrator because he is the transgressor. Allah said: 'So whoever transgresses against you, then transgress against him in the same way as he transgressed against you' (2:194) except that the 'aqila bear it for him, and a slave has no 'aqila, so it is required in his liability as in the dhimmi and attaches to his person and sold for it as in injury to property. And our proof is that what is owed by injury against lives is the perpetrator's own life when possible, except that the right to the life may be by way of destruction as a punishment and may be by way of ownership. And a slave qualifies to have his life owed in both ways, so his life becomes owed to the victim to prevent waste, unless the master chooses ransom, then he may do so because it does not nullify the victim's right; rather the victim's objective is achieved by that, unlike destruction of property where the perpetrator's life is never owed. And because the original rule in the requirement of accidental injury is to distance it from the perpetrator due to his excuse and because accident is lifted legally, and attaches to the nearest people to him to lighten the burden on the mistaken one and to avoid overburdening, except that the slave's 'aqila what he transgressed...
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on a slave's accidental jinaaya, as stated in Kanz al-Daqa'iq, followed by the sharh from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. The matn states: for an accidental injury by a slave, the master may either surrender the slave (the victim is then owned by the injured party) or pay the full compensation (arsh). The sharh clarifies that the restriction to 'accidental' is relevant primarily for homicide, as intentional homicide of a free person requires qisas; for limbs, there is no qisas between slaves or between slaves and freemen regardless of intent. The passage then presents an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that the slave's liability attaches to his person (raqaba) meaning he must be sold to pay the compensation unless the master voluntarily pays. The practical difference (thamara) is that under Shafi'i, the victim can pursue the slave even after he becomes free; under Hanafi, the liability ends with the master's option. The dispute traces to the Companions: Ibn Abbas supports the Hanafi view; Umar and Ali support the Shafi'i view. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof: the general principle is that the perpetrator is liable for his transgression (Quran 2:194), but the 'aqila normally bear the responsibility; since a slave has no 'aqila, the liability remains on the slave himself, analogous to a dhimmi, and his person (raqaba) can be sold, just as property is sold to satisfy injury to property. The Hanafi proof: the right of the victim in homicide is the perpetrator's life itself, but this can be either by execution (qisas) or by ownership (tamlik). A slave's life can be taken in ownership; so the victim has a right to own the slave to prevent his life being wasted (hadar). However, the master has the option to ransom because this does not nullify the victim's right but achieves it through payment. Additionally, the original rule for accidental injury is to distance the liability from the mistaken person due to his excuse, so it attaches to the nearest people (the 'aqila). But for a slave, there is no 'aqila, so the liability attaches to the master as the closest person. The text ends abruptly, likely continuing the Hanafi argument.

Key Terms
جناية (jinaayah): An injury (tort) committed by or against someone, here specifically the accidental injury caused by a slave.
أرش (arsh): The fixed compensation for bodily injuries other than death, here used for the full value of the injury (including diyah equivalents).
رقبة (raqabah): Literally 'neck', meaning the person or the personal capacity of the slave, especially regarding liability and ownership.
عاقلة ('aqila): The group of paternal male relatives who bear diyah on behalf of the perpetrator in accidental homicide/injury.
خطأ (khata'): Accidental, unintentional act; opposite of 'amd (intentional).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what options does a master have when his slave commits an accidental injury?
A) The master must pay the full compensation; he cannot surrender the slave.
B) The master must surrender the slave; he cannot pay compensation.
C) The master may either surrender the slave to the victim or ransom him by paying full compensation.
D) The slave is sold and the proceeds given to the victim; the master has no role.

Key Takeaway
For a slave's accidental injury, the Hanafi school grants the master a choice between surrendering the slave (ownership transfers) or paying full compensation, a ruling contested by the Shafi'i school.

Sequential Liability for Wounds on a Mudabbar Mukatab Slave
Calculating liability for sequential wounds on a changeable-status slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability for each sequential wound on a mudabbar mukatab slave and apply the Hanafi rule that sarayah is cut off by change of status.

Core Idea
Each wound's liability depends on the slave's status at injury; later changes cut off earlier sarayah.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced ruling from al-Muhit and al-Dhakhirah on the liability for multiple head wounds on a slave who is both mudabbar (promised freedom after master's death) and mukatab (contracted for freedom). The ruling illustrates how Hanafi fiqh meticulously apportions compensation based on the slave's evolving legal status, treating each wound independently and recognizing that subsequent emancipation or kitabah severs the causal chain of earlier wounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slave's status at each wound
Determine whether the slave was fully owned, mudabbar, mukatab, or free at the time each wound was inflicted. Status directly determines the compensation.
Status changes: first two wounds on a regular slave, third on mudabbar/mukatab, fourth on free.
2
Determine liability for each wound
Apply Hanafi rules: for a regular slave, arsh (proportional to value); for mudabbar/mukatab, reduced amount (here half a tenth of value for first two, one third of value for third); for free, full diya.
The passage specifies exact fractions.
3
Apply the rule of sarayah cut-off
Note that the spread (sarayah) of earlier wounds is cut off by subsequent changes in status (emancipation, kitabah). So if death results from earlier wounds after status change, no liability for those wounds.
Key principle: sarayah is severed by change in ownership status.
4
Calculate combined liability when death occurs
If multiple wounds contribute to death, apportion liability based on the status at each wound. The text calculates for four wounds: first two no liability (sarayah cut), third one third of value, fourth full diya but shifted to third because death occurred with four wounds.
The calculation ensures no double compensation.
5
Source the ruling's authority
Recognize that this ruling is from al-Muhit and al-Dhakhirah, later Hanafi works (nawadir). Understand its place in the hierarchy relative to the six foundational books (Zahir al-Riwayah).
Authority weight: nawadir; not from the core six.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفعه بالجناية فيملكه أو فداه بأرشها فإن فداه فجنى فهي كالأولى فإن جنى جنايتين، الشاج نصف عشر قيمته وهو عبد صحيح وأما حكم الشجة الثالثة فإنه يضمن نصف عشر قيمته وهو مدبر مكاتب مشجوج شجتين وأما حكم الشجة الرابعة فإنه يضمن ثلث الدية ولا يضمن الأرش، وأما حكم النفس فلا شيء على الشاج بسراية الشجة الأولى والثانية لأن سرايتهما منقطعة عن الجناية بالعتق والكتابة ويضمن للشجة الثالثة ثلث قيمته وهو مدبر مكاتب مشجوج بأربع شجات ولا يضمن ثلث الدية وإن مات حراً لأن ابتداء الشجة لاقي الكتابة، وإنما يضمن ثلث قيمته لا ربعها لأن الجناية الأولى والثانية حكمهما واحد والشجة الرابعة لاقته وهو حر وموجبها الدية فبان بهذا، واتضح أن النفس إنما تلفت واعتباراً بثلاث جنايات ثلثها بالجناية الأولى وقد هدرت سرايتها وثلثها بالجناية الثالثة وسرايتها معتبرة فيضمن ثلث قيمته مشجوحاً بأربع شجاج لأن ثلاث شجاج منها ضمنها مرة فلا يضمن مرة أخرى، وما تلف بالشجة الرابعة يكون مضموناً على الشاج بالشجة الثالثة لأنه مات وهو منقوص بأربع شجات؛ كذا في المحيط مع اختصار. وفي الذخيرة: أم الولد إذا جنت جناية خطأ فالجواب فيها كالجواب في المدبر على التفصيل المتقدم اهـ.
He hands him over for the crime and thus owns him, or he ransoms him with its compensation. If he ransoms him and he commits another crime, it is like the first. If he commits two crimes, the third wound: he guarantees half a tenth of his value, while he is a mudabbar and mukatab who has been wounded with two wounds. As for the ruling of the fourth wound: he guarantees one third of the blood-money and does not guarantee the compensation. As for the ruling of the life: nothing is upon the wounder due to the spread of the first and second wounds, because their spread is cut off from the crime by emancipation and kitabah. He guarantees for the third wound one third of his value, while he is a mudabbar and mukatab who has been wounded with four wounds, and he does not guarantee one third of the blood-money even if he dies free, because the beginning of the wound met kitabah. He only guarantees one third of his value, not one quarter, because the first and second crimes are one in ruling, and the fourth wound met him while he was free, and its liability is the blood-money. So this becomes clear, and it is evident that the life was lost and considered as three crimes: one third due to the first crime and its spread is waived, one third due to the third crime and its spread is accounted for, so he guarantees one third of his value while wounded with four wounds, because three wounds of them he guaranteed once so he does not guarantee again. What is lost due to the fourth wound is guaranteed upon the wounder by the third wound because he died while deficient with four wounds. Thus in al-Muhit with abridgment. And in al-Dhakhirah: A umm walad if she commits an accidental crime, the answer is like the answer for the mudabbar with the preceding detail.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling (hukm) from al-Muhit and al-Dhakhirah, expanding on the scenario of a slave who is both mudabbar (promised freedom upon master's death) and mukatab (contracted for freedom) and suffers multiple head wounds (shajjaj). The text calculates the liability for each wound separately based on the slave's status at the time the wound was inflicted. The first two wounds occurred when the slave was a regular slave (full owner liability), but their effects (saraya) are cut off by subsequent emancipation and kitabah, so no liability if death occurs from their spread. The third wound occurred while he was mudabbar and mukatab, so the master guarantees one third of the slave's value (not full diya) because the slave's value is reduced. The fourth wound occurred when the slave was free (after completing kitabah), so it would require full diya, but since the death resulted from a combination, the liability for the fourth wound is shifted to the third wound's guarantor because the slave died with all four wounds. The calculation shows the precision of Hanafi fiqh in treating each act separately.

Key Terms
شجة (shajjah): A head wound with specific rulings in diyat; here classified by severity
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whom the master has promised freedom upon his death
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a contract of manumission (kitabah) to earn his freedom
سراية (sarayah): The spreading or extension of the wound to cause death later
أرش (arsh): Compensation for injuries less than full diya (blood-money) on slaves
دية (diyah): Full blood-money for loss of life or specific limbs

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudabbar mukatab slave who already has two wounds suffers a third wound, what does the master guarantee?
A) Full diya because the slave dies
B) One third of his value (qimah)
C) One quarter of his value
D) Nothing because the wounds are from different events

Key Takeaway
The liability for wounds on a changeable-status slave is determined per wound based on status at time of injury, and sarayah is cut off by subsequent status changes.

Master's Khiyar: Daf' or Fida' for Slave's Accidental Crime
Inter-madhhab dispute on master's khiyar with dalil analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the master's khiyar and explain the Shafi'i counter-position with their respective proofs.

Core Idea
Master chooses: hand over the slave or pay compensation – victim's right is preserved either way.

When a slave accidentally harms someone, Islamic law must balance the victim's right to compensation with the master's property rights. The Hanafi school grants the master a choice (khiyar) to either surrender the slave (who then becomes the victim's property) or pay the compensation (arsh). This lesson unpacks this ruling, contrasts it with the Shafi'i view that the slave must be sold, and examines the proofs from the Quran, Sunnah, and rational analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a slave's accidental crime (jinayah khat'). The ruling: the master has a binding choice (khiyar) between handing over the slave (daf') or ransoming him (fida') by paying the arsh.
The khoir is wajib once selected; if the master chooses and does not execute, the other option lapses.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi proof is based on the principle that for bodily harms, the perpetrator's person can be owed either as punishment or as ownership. The slave is capable of being owned, so the master's choice preserves the victim's right.
The text cites the verse 'Faman i'tada 'alaykum' (2:194) as a general basis for equivalence, but the main argument is analogical.
3
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Al-Shafi'i holds that the slave must be sold to pay the compensation unless the master pays voluntarily. His proof: the crime attaches to the slave's neck, analogous to property crimes, and there is no 'aqilah for slaves.
Shafi'i relies on the idea that the slave's person is collateral for the debt.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes crimes against persons from property: the victim's right is to either receive compensation or take the slave as owner. The master's choice does not nullify the right but allows a practical option.
The text emphasizes that the slave's person is not owed in property crimes, so the analogy fails.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically: under Hanafi, the master can keep the slave by paying; under Shafi'i, the slave is sold unless the master pays, and the master cannot keep the slave without payment.
This affects the master's ability to retain valuable slaves.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (جنى عبد خطأ دفعه بالجناية فيملكه أو فداه بأرشها) أي إذا جنى العبد خطأ فمولاه بالخيار إن شاء دفعه إلى ولي الجناية فإن دفعه ملكه ولي الجناية، وإن شاء فداه بأرشها . وقوله «خطأ يحترز به من العمد وهذا التقييد إنما يفيد إذا كانت الجناية على النفس لأنها إن كانت عمداً توجب القصاص، وأما إذا كانت على الأطراف لا يفيد التقييد به إذا لا يجري القصاص فيها بين العبيد وبين الأحرار والعبيد. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : جناية العبد تتعلق برقبته يباع فيها إلا أن يقضى المولى الأرش. وثمرة الخلاف تظهر في اتباع الجاني عنده يتبع لا في حالة الرق ولا بعد الحرية والمسألة مختلفة بين الصحابة رضي الله عنهم؛ عنه ولا عاقلة للعبد فيجب في ذمته كما في الذمي ويتعلق برقبته ويباع فيه كما في الجناية على المال ولنا أن المستحق بالجناية على النفوس نفس الجاني إذا أمكن إلا أن استحقاق النفس قد يكون بطريق الإتلاف عقوبة بذلك بخلاف إتلاف المال فإنه لا يستحق به نفس الجاني أبداً، ولأن الأصل في موجب الجناية خطأ أن يتباعد عن الجاني لكونه معذوراً ولكون الخطأ مرفوعاً شرعاً ويتعلق بأقرب الناس إليه تخفيفاً عن المخطئ وتوقياً عن الإجحاف إلا أن عاقلة العبد مولاه لأن العبد يستنصر به وباعتبار النصرة تتحمل العاقلة حتى تجب الدية على أهل الديوان فيجب ضمان جنايته على المولى بخلاف الذمي فإنهم لا يتناصرون فيما بينهم فلا عاقلة لهم فيجب في ذمته صيانة عن الهدر وبخلاف الجناية على المال لأن العاقلة لا تعقل المال إلا أن المولى يخير بين الدفع والفداء لأنه واحد واختلف في الموجب الأصلي قال التمرتاشي : الصحيح أن الأصل هو الدية أو الأرش لكن للمولى أن يختار الدفع وفي إثبات الخيرة نوع تخفيف في حقه كيلا يستأصل فيخير لأن التخيير مفيد. وقال غيره : الواجب الأصلي هو الدفع في الصحيح ولهذا يسقط الواجب بموت العبد الجاني قبل الاختيار لفوات محل
