Multiple Crimes by a Slave: Master's Option and Distribution Among Victims
Ruling on master's option and distribution for multiple crimes by slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the master's option when a slave commits multiple crimes and apply the Hanafi rule that multiple victims' rights attach cumulatively to the slave.

Core Idea
Multiple rights attach to the same slave: the master must ransom for each crime, and victims share the slave proportionally.

When a slave commits multiple crimes, does the master have a single obligation or multiple? The Hanafi school rules that each crime gives rise to a separate right attaching to the slave's neck. The master must either hand over the slave to all victims or pay the full indemnity for each crime. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, including the key analogy to multiple creditors and the distinction from a pledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The slave has committed multiple crimes. The ruling: the master has an option to either hand over the slave or pay ransom (fidyah) for each crime. If the master frees the slave unknowingly, he is liable for the lesser of the slave's value and the total indemnity.
The indemnity (arsh) is the depreciation in value due to the injury.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is qiyas (analogical reasoning). The text compares the slave to a debtor with multiple creditors: each creditor's claim attaches without preventing others. Similarly, each victim's right attaches to the slave's neck.
3
Analyze the qiyas: asl, far', illah
Asl (original case): a debtor with multiple creditors, where each can claim from the debtor's assets. Far' (new case): a slave with multiple crimes. Illah (effective cause): the attachment of one right does not prevent attachment of another right to the same asset.
The illah is extracted by tashbīh (likeness) – both involve rights attaching to a thing.
4
Address the objection (pledge analogy)
An objection likens the slave to a pledge (rahn), where only one creditor's right attaches. The Hanafi response: a pledge is treated as actual fulfillment (istīfā') in legal construct, so it becomes like actual receipt. In crimes, there is no such fulfillment – only attachment of rights.
Thus the two cases are distinguished, and the multiple-creditor analogy prevails.
5
Determine the practical outcome
When the master hands over the slave to the victims, they divide him (i.e., his value or sale proceeds) according to the proportion of their respective claims. Each victim gets the amount equal to the indemnity for their injury relative to the total indemnity.
This ensures proportional justice among all victims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دفعه بهما أفداه بأرشهما فإن أعتقه غير عالم بالجناية ضمن الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش يمنع تعلق جناية، ولو جنى قبل أن تختار في الأولى شيئاً أو جنى جنايتين دفع دفعة واحدة، ولو جنايات قيل لمولاه إما أن تدفعه أو تفديه بأرش كل واحدة من الجنايات لأن تعلق الأولى برقبته لا يمنع تعلق الثانية بها كالمديون لأقوام أو لواحد ألا تری أن ملك المولى لا يمنع الجناية فحق المجني عليه أولى أن لا يمنع بخلاف الرهن حيث لا يتعلق به حق غيره من الغرماء، والفرق أن الرهن إيفاء واستيفاء حكماً فصار كالاستيفاء حقيقة، فأما الجناية فليس فيها إلا تعلق الحق لولي الأولى وذلك لا يمنع تعلق حق آخر به ثم إذا دفعه إليه اقتسموه على قدر حقوقهم وحق كل واحد منهم أرش جنايته .
He hands him over for both or ransoms him with the indemnity for both. If he frees him unaware of the crime, he is liable for the lesser of his value and the indemnity. It prevents attachment of the crime. If he commits a crime before he chooses anything in the first, or commits two crimes, he is handed over in one go. If multiple crimes, it is said to his master: either hand him over or ransom him with the indemnity for each crime, because the attachment of the first to his neck does not prevent the attachment of the second, like a debtor to several creditors or to one. Do you not see that the master's ownership does not prevent the crime, so the right of the victim is more entitled not to be prevented, unlike a pledge where the right of other creditors does not attach. The difference is that the pledge is a fulfilment and receipt by way of legal construct, so it becomes like actual receipt. As for the crime, it is only the attachment of the right of the first guardian, and that does not prevent the attachment of another right to it. Then when he hands him over to them, they divide him according to their rights, and the right of each of them is the indemnity for his crime.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling when a slave commits multiple crimes (جنايات). The master has an option: either hand over the slave to the victims or pay the full indemnity (أرش) for each crime. If the master frees the slave without knowledge of the crime, he is liable for the lesser of the slave's value and the total indemnity. The key principle is that the attachment of one victim's right to the slave's neck does not prevent another victim's right from attaching; this is analogous to multiple creditors of a debtor. The text distinguishes this from the case of a pledge (رهن), where only one creditor can attach rights to the pledged property because a pledge is treated as actual receipt (استيفاء). The victims who receive the slave divide him proportionally based on their respective claims.

Key Terms
الأرش (al-arsh): The indemnity or compensation due for a crime against a slave's body; it is the difference in value before and after the injury
تعلق (taʿalluq): The attachment of a legal right to a specific object (here, the slave's neck as security for the crime)
الرهن (ar-rahn): A pledge; an item given as security for a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a slave who commits multiple crimes, what does the master have the option to do?
A) Hand over the slave or pay ransom for each crime
B) Pay a single ransom for all crimes
C) Free the slave and pay nothing
D) Hand over the slave for only the first crime

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a slave's multiple crimes create separate rights that attach cumulatively to his neck, requiring the master to either hand him over or pay ransom for each, with victims sharing proportionally.

Master's Option to Ransom After Slave's Intentional Killing: Effect of One vs Two Guardians
Ruling on master's option when slave commits intentional killing with one or two guardians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the master's choice when a slave kills a man with one or two guardians, and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute in the riwaya of Kitāb al-Durr.

Core Idea
When a slave kills, the master's choice to ransom for one guardian binds him for all, because the right belongs to the deceased's estate.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq tackles a specific nuance in diyat (blood money) law: what happens when a slave commits intentional killing and the victim has multiple guardians? The master has the option to ransom his slave or hand him over. But can he split the choice? The text presents the majority Hanafi position and a minority riwaya, revealing the underlying property logic that drives the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the master's option
The base case: a slave intentionally kills a free man. The victim's guardian (or guardians) demand ransom (al-fidāʾ). The master may choose to ransom the slave (keep him) or let him go for qisas. Understand that 'ransom' here means paying the value of the slave to the victim's estate.
2
Understand the ruling when there is one guardian
If the master tries to ransom only half the slave (e.g., pay half his value), he is forced to ransom the whole. Why? Because dividing the slave would cause harm to the master (he loses partial property and still owes the rest), and the law prevents this harm.
The key concept: 'ضرر' (harm) to the master is avoided by forcing an all-or-nothing choice.
3
Understand the ruling when there are two guardians (majority view)
With two guardians, the master's choice to ransom for one guardian makes him a chooser for the other as well. The text gives a proof: the right to the crime's consequence (mūjib al-jināya) belongs first to the deceased, then transfers to heirs. Since the deceased had one unified right, the master's choice cannot be split.
The proof rests on Hanafi rules of inheritance: the estate is considered owned by the deceased before distribution to heirs.
4
Identify the minority riwaya and its basis
The riwaya of Kitāb al-Durr says the master does NOT become bound for the other guardian. Reason: ownership of the crime's consequence goes directly to the master (since the deceased cannot own after death), so the two guardians are separate claimants; splitting is allowed.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the majority position is relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قتل عبد رجلاً عمداً وله ولي واحد فطلب الفداء فاختار المولى عن نصف العبد يصير مختاراً للفداء عن الكل لأن في التفريق ضرراً عليه فلا يتمكن المولى من ذلك فصار مختاراً للفداء عن الكل ضرورة، وإن كان له وليان فاختار الفداء في أحدهما يصير مختاراً للفداء في حق الآخر في عامة الروايات لأن المستحق لموجب الجناية هو الميت لأن الجناية وردت على حقه وأمكن إثبات الملك لموجب الجناية لأن بعد الموت تبقى التركة على الحكم الملك ولهذا لا تنفذ وصاياه وتقضى منها ديونه فوقع الملك للميت أولاً ثم انتقل إلى الوراث وكان المستحق لموجب الجناية هذا فيصير مختاراً للفداء من الكل ضرورة. وفي رواية كتاب الدر لا يصير مختاراً لأن الملك في موجب الجناية يثبت للمولى ابتداء لأن الميت ليس بأهل للملك فكان المستحق للجناية اثنين فالتفريق لا يلتحق.
A slave intentionally killed a man who has one guardian, and he demanded ransom. The master chose [to ransom] half of the slave; he becomes a chooser for ransom for the whole because dividing [the slave] would cause him harm, so he is unable to do that, so he becomes a chooser for ransom for the whole by necessity. If there are two guardians, and he chooses ransom for one of them, he becomes a chooser for ransom for the other's share in the majority of reports, because the one entitled to the result of the crime (mūjib al-jināya) is the deceased, since the crime struck his right, and it is possible to establish ownership for the result of the crime, because after death the estate remains under the rule of ownership. For this reason, his bequests are not executed and his debts are paid from it. So ownership is established for the deceased first, then transfers to the heirs, and the one entitled to the result of the crime is this [deceased], so he becomes a chooser for ransom for the whole by necessity. In a riwaya of Kitāb al-Durr, he does not become a chooser because ownership in the result of the crime is established for the master initially, since the deceased is not capable of ownership, so the entitled parties are two, and division is not possible.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage deals with intentional killing by a slave where the victim has one or two guardians (وليّان). If there is one guardian and the master chooses to ransom half the slave, he is forced to ransom the whole because splitting the slave (e.g., handing over half) would harm the master (as he would lose part of his property and still owe the rest). With two guardians, the majority of riwayat hold that choosing ransom for one guardian makes the master choose ransom for the other as well, because the right to the crime's consequence (موجب الجناية) belongs to the deceased victim first, then to the heirs. Therefore, the master's choice binds him to ransom the whole slave. However, a riwaya from Kitāb al-Durr states the opposite: the master does not become a chooser for the other guardian because the ownership of the crime's consequence goes directly to the master initially (since the deceased cannot own), so the entitled parties are two separate guardians, and splitting is allowed.

Key Terms
موجب الجناية (mūjib al-jināya): The liability or consequence resulting from the crime; here it refers to the claim for ransom or the value of the slave that is due to the victim's estate
الفداء (al-fidā’): Ransom paid by the master to retain the slave instead of handing him over to the victim's guardians

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of riwayat, if there are two guardians and the master chooses ransom for one of them, what is the consequence?
A) He only ransoms for that one guardian
B) He becomes bound to ransom for the other guardian as well
C) He must hand over the slave
D) He is free from both

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a slave kills with multiple guardians, the master's option to ransom is indivisible because the right to the crime's consequence belongs to the deceased's single estate.

Liability for Freeing a Slave Unaware of His Crime
States the liability of the master who frees a slave without knowing the crime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master frees a slave unaware of the crime and calculate the compensation as the lesser of the slave's value or the arsh.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave unaware of his crime: master pays the lesser of value or blood money.

This lesson presents a concise ruling from the Hanafi matn on a specific scenario in the law of jinayat. When a master frees his slave without knowing that the slave has committed a crime, the master becomes liable to pay the lesser of two amounts: the slave's market value or the arsh (blood money for the injury). This ruling balances the victim's right to compensation with the master's ignorance, preventing excessive burden. You will learn to apply this rule through examples and calculations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the master freeing a slave who has committed a crime (jinaya), while the master is unaware of the crime. The ruling is that the master guarantees (daman) the lesser of two amounts: the slave's value (qimah) or the arsh (blood money for the injury).
2
Understand the two amounts
The slave's value (qimah) is his market price at the time of freeing. The arsh is the blood money due for the specific injury or crime committed by the slave. Both are fixed amounts determined by the circumstances.
Arsh varies depending on the severity of the crime.
3
Apply the rule: always pay the lesser
The master must pay whichever is smaller: if the value is less than the arsh, he pays the value; if the arsh is less, he pays the arsh. This ensures the victim is compensated without overburdening the master.
The rationale is to protect the master who acted in ignorance.
4
Note the adjacent ruling
If the master knew of the crime at the time of freeing, the ruling is different: he would be liable for the full arsh (blood money) because his act of freeing intentionally removed the slave from liability. This case is not in the text but is derived from the same principle.
Ignorance mitigates liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (فإن أعتقه غير عالم بالجناية ضمن الأقل من قيمته ومن الأرش) يعني لو أعتق الجاني ولم يعلم بها ضمن الأقل من القيمة ومن الأرش.
He, may Allah have mercy on him, said: (If he frees him unaware of the crime, he guarantees the lesser of his value and the arsh) Meaning: if he frees the offender and did not know of the crime, he guarantees the lesser of the value and the arsh.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a concise ruling directly from the matn (original text) with a brief explanation from the commentator. The ruling is: if a master frees his slave without knowing that the slave has committed a crime (jinayah), the master becomes liable to pay the lesser of two amounts: the slave's market value (qimah) or the arsh (blood money for the injury). This ensures that the victim is compensated without overburdening the master, who acted in ignorance. The commentator simply clarifies the meaning. This is a clear, memorizable ruling typical of the Hanafi texts.

Key Terms
عتق (itq): The act of freeing a slave by the master.
قيمة (qimah): The market value of the slave at the time of freeing.
أرش (arsh): Blood money or compensation for an injury caused by the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 1000 dirhams
B) 500 dirhams
C) 1500 dirhams
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
When a master frees a slave unaware of the slave's crime, the master must pay compensation equal to the lesser of the slave's value or the blood money (arsh) for the injury.

6 min
Master's Choice After Victim's Death: Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on master's option after victim dies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on whether a master gets a new choice when the victim dies after the master chose for the wound, and explain the basis of each in qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
When a wound becomes fatal, does the master regain choice? Muhammad (istihsan) says yes; Abu Yusuf (qiyas) says no.

This lesson examines a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of a subsequent death on the master's election. After a slave inflicts a wound, the master may choose to hand over the slave or pay compensation. But if the victim later dies, does that choice remain binding, or does a new choice arise? The two great disciples of Abu Hanifa, Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf, differed on this point.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
A slave wounds a free man; the master exercises his choice (either surrendering the slave or paying fida' for the wound). However, before the choice is executed, the wound proves fatal and the victim dies.
The case involves a change in the nature of liability from arsh (wound compensation) to diyah (blood money for death).
2
Identify the two contrary positions
Imam Muhammad: the master is given a new choice – he may now either surrender the slave or pay the diyah of the victim. Imam Abu Yusuf: the master's original choice stands; he must pay the diyah without any new option.
Both positions are within the Hanafi school; this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the reasoning of each position
Muhammad relies on istihsan (juristic preference): the legal nature of the case has changed from a wound to a death, so the previous choice should not bind. Abu Yusuf relies on qiyas: the master's election is a binding act; the later death does not retroactively cancel it.
Istihsan allows departure from a clear qiyas for a more equitable result.
4
Trace the later development of the school
The commentator notes that Abu Yusuf himself subsequently reverted from qiyas to istihsan in these cases. This indicates that Muhammad's view became the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The mature Hanafi school follows istihsan here.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Muhammad's view, the master may still surrender the slave to avoid paying full diyah. Under Abu Yusuf's view, the master must pay diyah whether or not he surrenders the slave; the slave's value is deducted from diyah only if surrendered.
The practical difference is whether the master retains a choice to hand over the slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا جرح العبد رجلاً فاختار مات المجروح خير مرة أخرى عند محمد استحساناً، وعند أبي يوسف عليه الدية ولا يخير قياساً. وهي المسائل التي رجع فيها أبو يوسف رحمه الله إلى الاستحسان.
And if the slave wounds a man, and (the master) chooses, then the wounded man dies, he is given the choice again according to Muhammad by istihsan, and according to Abu Yusuf the diyah is upon him and he is not given the choice by qiyas. These are the issues in which Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, reverted from qiyas to istihsan.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between two of the leading disciples of Abu Hanifa: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani and Imam Abu Yusuf. The issue arises when a master has already made his choice (either to hand over the slave or pay fida') for a wound inflicted by his slave, but then the victim dies from that wound before the master has acted. According to Muhammad, by istihsan (juristic preference), the master is given a new choice, because the nature of the liability has changed from a wound (with arsh) to death (with diyah). According to Abu Yusuf, by qiyas (analogy), the master's initial choice is final, and he must pay the full diyah for the death; no new choice is given. The commentator notes that these are cases where Abu Yusuf later adopted istihsan, indicating the mature position of the madhhab.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of reasoning that leaves a clear qiyas for a more appropriate ruling.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning from an established case (asl) to a new case (far') based on a common effective cause (illa).
دية (diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of a murder victim.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a wound or injury, graded by severity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf's qiyas, if a victim dies after the master chose for the wound, what is the ruling?
A) The master is given a new choice.
B) The master must pay the diyah without a new choice.
C) The master is free from liability.
D) The master must pay only the arsh of the wound.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a victim's death changes the nature of liability, istihsan grants a new choice to the master, overriding the qiyas of finality.

Freeing a Slave with Knowledge and Death of the Victim: Qiyas vs. Istihsan in Diyat
Analyzes qiyas vs. istihsan in diyat liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school, through istihsan, does not consider the master's freeing with knowledge as an automatic choice of diyah, and distinguish this from the qiyas argument.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave with knowledge of a wound does not automatically bind the master to diyah because he only consented to a small payment, not the full blood money.

In Hanafi diyat law, when a slave commits a crime and the master frees him knowing the victim's injury, a question arises if the victim later dies: does this act signify acceptance of diyah? The passage examines two opposing legal arguments—qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference)—and settles on istihsan to protect the master from unexpected heavy liability.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
A master frees his slave knowing the slave's crime (a wound), then the victim dies. The issue is whether this freeing constitutes a choice of diyah.
2
Identify the qiyas argument
The master's choice of keeping the slave by paying arsh is analogous to pardoning the wound (al-afw 'an al-jirahah), which includes pardoning subsequent death because siraayah is inseparable.
3
Understand the istihsan rebuttal
The master acted on the assumption the wound would not be fatal; forcing diyah would impose a large payment he never consented to, causing hardship.
4
Compare the consequences
Under qiyas the master pays diyah; under istihsan he gets a new choice (khiyar) and is not automatically liable.
5
Note the mufta bihi
The Hanafi school adopts istihsan; the master is not considered a chooser of diyah merely by freeing with knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أعتقه وهو يعلم ثم مات المجروح كان مختاراً للدية إن كان خطأ وجه القياس أنه اختار أرش الجراحة فيكون اختياراً لأرشها ما يحدث ويتولد عنها كالعفو عن الجراحة ويكون عفواً عنها وعما يحدث منها لأن السراية لا تنفك عن الجناية فيكون اختيار الأصل اختياراً للتبع المتولد منه ضرورة لأنه صار قاتلاً بتلك الجراحة فظهر أنه اختار إمساك العبد بعد القتل وهو عالم بالقتل كما لو أعتق العبد بعد الجراحة. وجه الاستحسان أن المولى إنما اختار إمساك العبد بمال قليل على حساب أن الجراحة لا تسري فبعد الموت لو لزمه لزمه حكم الاختيار بمال كثير وهو دية واختيار الإنسان إمساك العبد بمال قليل لا يكون اختياراً منه بأداء مال كثير لأنه غير راض به فلو لزمه تضرر به فوجب أن لا يلزمه حكم الاختيار بالدية بخلاف ما لو أعتقه بعد الجراحة ثم مات لأنه لم ينص على اختيار العبد بمال قليل بل اختار إمساك العبد مطلقاً.
And if he frees him while knowing, then the wounded man dies, he is considered a chooser of diyah if it was manslaughter. The basis of qiyas is that he chose the arsh of the wound, so it is a choice for its arsh and what develops and arises from it, like pardoning the wound, and it becomes a pardon for it and for what arises from it, because the progression (siraayah) is inseparable from the crime, so choosing the origin is necessarily a choice for the subsequent consequence, because he became a killer by that wound, so it appears that he chose to retain the slave after the killing while knowing of the killing, as if he freed the slave after the wound. The basis of istihsan is that the master only chose to retain the slave with a small amount of money on the assumption that the wound would not progress. After death, if it were binding on him, a choice with a large amount of money would be binding, namely the diyah, and a person's choice to retain the slave with a small amount of money is not a choice to pay a large amount, because he is not content with it. If it were binding, he would be harmed, so it is necessary that the ruling of choice for the diyah not be binding on him, unlike if he freed him after the wound and then died, because he did not specify retaining the slave with a small amount of money, but rather chose to retain the slave absolutely.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where a master frees his slave, being aware of the crime, and then the victim later dies from the injury. The question is whether this freeing constitutes an implicit choice of fida' for the diyah (blood money for death) after death. The text presents two opposing arguments: qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference). According to qiyas, the master's initial choice of retaining the slave by paying arsh means he also chose what would inevitably follow from the wound, i.e., death, so he must now pay diyah. This is compared to pardoning the wound, which would also pardon death arising from it. According to istihsan, the master chose to keep the slave with a small payment (arsh) based on the assumption that the wound would not become fatal. Imposing the full diyah after death would be an unfair burden on him, as he never agreed to such a large payment. Therefore, freeing with knowledge does not automatically make him liable for diyah; a new choice must be given. This demonstrates the Hanafi method of balancing strict analogy with equity.

Key Terms
سراية (siraayah): The progression or spread of an injury leading to death.
أرش (arsh): Compensation for a wound short of diyah.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid for causing death, either intentionally or unintentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to istihsan, why is the master not considered a chooser of diyah if he frees his slave with knowledge and the victim later dies?
A) Because he did not intend to keep the slave.
B) Because he only consented to a small payment (arsh), not the full diyah.
C) Because the death was not caused by the wound.
D) Because he was unaware of the crime.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, through istihsan, protects the master from automatic liability for diyah when he frees a slave with knowledge of a non-fatal wound that later becomes fatal.

Partial Choice of Fida' with Multiple Heirs: Majority vs. Kitab al-Durr
Discusses effects of partial choice of fida' by the master with multiple heirs, with two differing narrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the majority Hanafi position on partial fida' with multiple heirs and explain the opposing narration from Kitab al-Durr, including the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, partial choice of fida' extends to the whole claim to prevent splitting the slave, per the majority view.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq delves into a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a master's partial choice of fida' (ransom) when there are multiple heirs constitutes a choice for the entire claim. The majority of narrations hold that it does, based on the principle that the crime's consequence vests in the deceased first, making the right singular. A minority narration from Kitab al-Durr disagrees, arguing that the master is the initial owner of the claim, so the heirs are separate claimants. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how differences in usul (legal theory) produce different furu' (positive law) outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal question
Read the beginning of the passage: a slave kills a man intentionally. There is either one heir or two heirs. The master chooses fida' for part of the slave or for only one heir. The question is: does this partial choice make the master a chooser for the whole?
The key variable is the number of heirs and the nature of the claim.
2
State the majority position and its dalil
In 'ammah al-riwayat (most narrations), the master becomes a chooser for the whole. The reason: the right to the consequence of the crime (the claim) vests in the deceased first, then transfers to heirs. Thus it is a single right; splitting it would cause harm (darar) to the master. Therefore partial choice necessitates full choice.
The dalil is based on the vesting of ownership in the deceased's estate.
3
State the minority position and its dalil
In the narration of Kitab al-Durr, the master does not become a chooser for the whole. The reason: the claim vests in the master initially, because the deceased is not capable of owning. Thus the claimants (heirs) are separate; partial choice does no harm since the master is not liable for the other heir's share.
This is a difference in the initial attribution of the right.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the majority, the master pays fida' for the entire diyah (full blood money). Under the minority, he pays only for the portion he chose, and the other heir retains the right to demand the slave or fida' for his share.
This affects the master's liability and the heirs' rights.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The passage indicates that the majority view is relied upon ('ammah al-riwayat). Later Hanafi works also adopt this as the mufta bihi because it prevents harm and is consistent with the structure of inheritance.
Know which view is operative for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قتل عبد رجلاً عمداً وله ولي واحد فطلب الفداء فاختار المولى عن نصف العبد يصير مختاراً للفداء عن الكل لأن في التفريق ضرراً عليه فلا يتمكن المولى من ذلك فصار مختاراً للفداء عن الكل ضرورة، وإن كان له وليان فاختار الفداء في أحدهما يصير مختاراً للفداء في حق الآخر في عامة الروايات لأن المستحق لموجب الجناية هو الميت لأن الجناية وردت على حقه وأمكن إثبات الملك لموجب الجناية لأن بعد الموت تبقى التركة على الحكم الملك ولهذا لا تنفذ وصاياه وتقضى منها ديونه فوقع الملك للميت أولاً ثم انتقل إلى الوراث وكان المستحق لموجب الجناية هذا فيصير مختاراً للفداء من الكل ضرورة. وفي رواية كتاب الدر لا يصير مختاراً لأن الملك في موجب الجناية يثبت للمولى ابتداء لأن الميت ليس بأهل للملك فكان المستحق للجناية اثنين فالتفريق لا يلتحق بأحدهما ضرر لم يكن مستحقاً عليه. وفي قتل الخطأ لو كان الولي واحداً فاختار الفداء في النصف يكون اختياراً للفداء في حق الآخر ما دام العبد قائماً لأن حقهما ثبت في العبد متفرقاً مشتركاً. وإذا مات العبد قبل أن يدفع النصف إلى الآخر يصير مختاراً للفداء لأن الحق ثبت للمقتول، ولو صالح أحدهما على نصف العبد خير المولى والولي المدفوع إليه بين أن يدفعا نصف العبد إلى الثاني أو يفديا لأن الجناية انقلبت مالاً والعبد في ملكهما فيعتبر بمال لو جنى جناية خطأ والعبد ملكهما يخير بين الدفع والفداء فكذا هذا لأن العبد فرغ من نصف الجناية بالصلح وبقي مشغولاً بالنصف فثبت لهما الخيار في النصف. وإن صالح أحدهما عن جميع العبد قيل للشريك ادفع نصفه إلى أخيك أو أفده لأنه انتقل الملك إليه ونصفه مشغول بالجناية، ولو قتلت أمة رجلاً عمداً وله وليان فصالح المولى أحدهما على ولدها صار مختاراً للفداء في نصيب الآخر فيفديه بنصف الدية. وذكر في كتاب الدرر لا يصير مختاراً للفداء . ولو صالح أحدهما في ثلث الأمة كان الثاني له خيار أن يدفعه أو يفديه. وفي الجامع والدرر : لا يكون منه اختياراً وجه هذه الرواية أنه سوى بين الدفع والفداء في البعض وذلك لأن الملك يقع للميت أولاً ثم ينتقل إلى الوارث لما بينا فكان ملك الميت أصلاً وملك الوارث بناء عليه فيكون المستحق للجناية واحداً، فاختيار الدفع والفداء في البعض يكون اختياراً في الكل لئلا يتفرق الملك على المستحق.
If a slave kills a man intentionally and he has one heir, and he (the heir) demands fida', and the master chooses half of the slave, he becomes a chooser of fida' for the whole, because splitting him would cause harm, so the master cannot do that, so he becomes a chooser of fida' for the whole by necessity. And if he (the victim) has two heirs, and he (the master) chooses fida' for one of them, he becomes a chooser of fida' for the other in the majority of narrations, because the one entitled to the consequence of the crime is the deceased, because the crime was upon his right, and it is possible to establish ownership for the consequence of the crime because after death the estate remains on the ruling of ownership, and for this reason his bequests are not carried out and his debts are paid from it. So ownership occurs for the deceased first, then it transfers to the inheritors, and the one entitled to the consequence of the crime is this, so he becomes a chooser of fida' for the whole by necessity. And in a narration of Kitab al-Durr, he does not become a chooser, because ownership in the consequence of the crime is established for the master initially, because the deceased is not capable of ownership, so the ones entitled to the crime are two, and splitting does not cause harm to one of them that was not due. And in manslaughter, if the heir is one and he chooses fida' for half, it becomes a choice of fida' for the other half as long as the slave remains, because their right is established in the slave separately and jointly. And if the slave dies before he hands over the half to the other, he becomes a chooser of fida', because the right is established for the slain. And if one of them (the heirs) settles for half of the slave, the master and the heir who was paid to are given the choice between handing over the half to the second or ransoming because the crime has turned into property and the slave is in their ownership, so it is considered like property: if a slave commits a manslaughter and the slave is owned by them jointly, they are given the choice between handing over and ransoming; similarly here, because the slave is freed from half the crime by the settlement and remains occupied with half, so the choice in the half is established for them. And if one of them settles for the entire slave, it is said to the partner: hand over his half to your brother or ransom it, because ownership has transferred to him and half of him is occupied with the crime. And if a female slave kills a man intentionally and he has two heirs, and the master settles with one of them for her child, he becomes a chooser of fida' for the share of the other, so he ransoms it with half the diyah. And it is mentioned in Kitab al-Durar that he does not become a chooser of fida'. And if one of them settles for a third of the female slave, the second has the choice to hand over or ransom. And in al-Jami' and al-Durar: it does not constitute a choice. The basis of this narration is that he made no distinction between handing over and ransoming in part, and that is because ownership occurs for the deceased first then transfers to the heir as we stated, so the deceased's ownership is original and the heir's ownership is based on it, so the one entitled to the crime is one. Therefore, choosing to hand over or ransom in part becomes a choice for the whole to prevent splitting the ownership among the one entitled.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This long passage deals with the implications of partial choice by the master when there are one or two heirs (or guardians) of the victim. It covers both intentional killing (amd) and manslaughter (qatl al-khata'). The core issue is whether choosing fida' (ransom) for part of the slave's value or for only one heir's share constitutes a choice for the entire claim. The passage presents two main views: the majority opinion (in 'ammah al-riwayat) holds that partial choice extends to the whole to avoid splitting the slave and causing harm to the master. The alternative narration from Kitab al-Durr and Kitab al-Durar holds that the master does not become a chooser for the whole because ownership of the claim initially goes to the master (not the deceased), making the heirs separate claimants; partial choice is not harmful. The passage then discusses specific scenarios: settlement for half the slave, settlement by one heir for the whole slave (where the other heir gets a choice), settlement for a child of a female slave, and settlement for a third. The commentary explains the reasoning behind each, emphasizing the underlying principle of whether the deceased's estate vests ownership in the deceased first or directly in the master. This is a detailed technical discussion typical of later Hanafi juristic refinement (tahqiq).

Key Terms
فداء (fida'): Ransom; the payment made by the master to retain the slave instead of handing him over to the victim's heirs.
ولي (wali): Guardian or heir of the victim who has the right to claim retribution or compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He only pays for that heir's share.
B) He becomes a chooser for the whole, paying full diyah.
C) The other heir loses his right.
D) The master must hand over the slave.

Key Takeaway
When the master partially chooses fida' with multiple heirs, the majority view treats it as choice for the whole to avoid harm, while the minority view treats the heirs as separate claimants.

Rulings When Slave Dies or Is Killed Before Choice
Summarizes consequences if the slave dies, is killed by master, or by third party before choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for each scenario where a slave dies or is killed before the master's choice, and apply the correct rule to new cases.

Core Idea
A slave's death before choice voids the crime; the master's killing implies choice of arsh; third-party killing gives qisas or value to victims.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a set of scenarios following a slave's commission of a crime. The master has not yet chosen between handing over the slave or paying fida'. The slave then dies or is killed by various parties. The rulings are precise: each outcome preserves the rights of all involved. Understanding these is crucial for the law of diyat and slavery in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
The slave has committed a crime (intentional or accidental) and the master has not yet exercised his choice between daf' (handing over the slave) and fida' (ransom).
2
Recognize the different causes of death
Causes include: natural death, killed by master, killed by stranger (intentional/accidental), and killed by another slave.
Each cause triggers a different ruling.
3
State the consequence for each cause
For natural death: crime void, master pays nothing. Master kills: master chooses arsh (must pay blood money for original injury). Stranger kills intentionally: crime void, master has qisas. Stranger kills accidentally: master takes value of slave and pays it to original victim's guardians; disposal does not imply choosing arsh. Another slave kills: guardian of original victim chooses daf' or fida'; if slave handed over, he stands in for the dead slave, and master's disposal implies choice of fida'.
4
Note the special case of the substituted slave
When a killer slave is handed over to the master of the original offending slave, he takes the place of the dead slave 'in flesh and blood'. Any disposal (sale, freeing) by the master is considered a choice of fida'.
This ensures consistency in the master's election.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا مات العبد قبل أن يختار المولى شيئاً بطلت الجناية عمداً كانت أو خطأ ولا يؤخذ المولى بشيء، فإن لم يمت ولكن قتله مولاه فإنه يصير مختاراً للأرش، فإن لم يقتله مولاه ولكن قتله أجنبي، فإن كان عمداً بطلت الجناية وللمولى أن يقتص، وإن كان خطأ يأخذ القيمة ثم يدفع تلك القيمة إلى أولياء الجناية حتى لو تصرف في تلك القيمة لا يصير مختاراً للأرش. وكذلك لو قتله عبد فخير الولي بين الدفع والفداء ويدفع إلى ولي الجناية، ولو دفع العبد إلى مولى العبد المقتول قام مقامه لحماً ودماً كأنه هو فيخير المولى بالفداء حتى لو تصرف في العبد المدفوع بالبيع أو بالعتق أو نحوه فإن يصير مختاراً للفداء.
And if the slave dies before the master chooses anything, the crime becomes void, whether intentional or accidental, and nothing is taken from the master. And if he does not die but his master kills him, then he becomes a chooser of the arsh. And if his master does not kill him but a stranger kills him: if it was intentional, the crime becomes void and the master has the right to retaliate; if it was accidental, he takes the value (of the slave) and then pays that value to the guardians of the crime, such that if he disposes of that value, he does not become a chooser of the arsh. Similarly, if a (another) slave kills him, the guardian (of the original victim) has the choice between handover and ransom, and he hands over to the guardian of the crime. And if he hands over the slave to the master of the slain slave, he takes his place in flesh and blood as if he were him, so the master (of the original slave) is given the choice of ransom, such that if he disposes of the delivered slave by sale or freeing or similar, he becomes a chooser of the fida'.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents a set of rulings for situations where the slave dies or is killed before the master has exercised his choice (between daf' and fida') for the crime the slave committed. The rulings vary based on who caused the slave's death. (1) Natural death: the crime is void; no liability on the master. (2) Master kills him: the master is considered to have chosen the arsh (i.e., he must pay the blood money for the original injury). (3) Stranger kills him intentionally: the crime becomes void, and the master can pursue qisas (retaliation) against the killer because the slave was his property. (4) Stranger kills him accidentally: the master takes the value of the slave from the killer and must pay that value to the original victim's guardians; if he uses the value himself, it does not count as choosing arsh. (5) Another slave kills him: the guardian of the original victim has the choice between demanding the slave or his fida', and the master of that other slave must hand over or pay. (6) If the original victim's guardian hands over the slave to the master of the original offending slave, that slave takes the place of the dead slave, and the master can then choose fida' for that slave; any disposal (sale, freeing) is a choice of fida'. These detailed rules ensure that the rights of all parties are preserved in various scenarios.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation in kind, here the master's right to exact retaliatory killing for the intentional killing of his slave.
أرش (arsh): A fixed amount of blood money paid for a specific injury or harm (not full diyah). Here, for the injury committed by the slave.
فداء (fida'): Ransom paid to redeem the slave from being handed over to the victim's guardians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a slave dies a natural death before the master chooses for the original crime?
A) The master must pay the arsh.
B) The crime is void and the master pays nothing.
C) The master must hand over the slave's corpse.
D) The master is given a new choice.

Key Takeaway
The death or killing of a slave before the master's choice triggers specific rules that protect the rights of both the master and the original crime victim.

Effect of Compromise with One Heir on Master's Choice in Slave's Jinaayah
Ruling on master's khiyar after compromise with one heir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a master compromises with one of multiple heirs after a slave's jinaayah and apply the rule of khiyar to the remaining heirs.

Core Idea
Compromise with one heir transforms the claim into property, making the master's choice apply jointly.

In cases where a slave commits jinaayah and has multiple heirs (awliyaa), the master initially has the choice (khiyar) between handing over the slave (daf') or paying ransom (fidaa). When the master compromises with one heir, the nature of the claim changes, affecting the choices of all parties. This lesson explores the detailed rulings derived from analogy to joint ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base scenario
Start with a slave who commits accidental killing (qatl al-khata') and has two guardians (awliyaa). The master has the choice to pay fidaa for half the slave or hand over the slave. Understand that the rights of the heirs are established in the slave jointly yet separately.
The text begins with 'In killing by mistake, if there is one guardian...' but the full scenario involves two guardians.
2
Analyze the effect of compromise for half the slave
When one guardian compromises for half the slave, the offense becomes a property claim. The master and the compromising guardian now jointly own the slave (since the guardian gets half ownership). Their joint ownership triggers a parallel to the rule for jointly-owned slaves who commit jinaayah: they have the choice between handing over their half or paying fidaa.
The text says 'because the offense has turned into property and the slave is in their joint ownership'.
3
Examine the case of compromise for the entire slave
If one guardian compromises for the whole slave, ownership transfers entirely to that guardian, but half the slave remains encumbered by the jinaayah. The other guardian is told to either hand over half or ransom it. This is because the compromising guardian now owns the entire slave, but half is still owed to the second guardian.
The text says 'it is said to the partner: hand over his half to your brother or ransom it'.
4
Consider the scenario of the slave's death
If the slave dies before the master pays the owed half to the other guardian, the master becomes bound to pay fidaa (ransom). This is because the right was for the deceased victim, and the option to hand over the slave no longer exists.
The text: 'if the slave dies before he pays the half to the other, he becomes chooser of ransom'.
5
Apply to the case of a female slave with a child
When a female slave kills intentionally and has two guardians, and the master compromises with one guardian concerning her child (walad), that compromise makes the master bound to ransom the share of the other guardian by paying half the blood-money (diyah). This is because the child is considered part of the slave's estate, and compromising transfers the obligation.
The text: 'he becomes chooser of ransom for the share of the other, so he ransoms him with half the blood-money'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بأحدهما ضرر لم يكن مستحقاً عليه. وفي قتل الخطأ لو كان الولي واحداً فاختار الفداء في النصف يكون اختياراً للفداء في حق الآخر ما دام العبد قائماً لأن حقهما ثبت في العبد متفرقاً مشتركاً. وإذا مات العبد قبل أن يدفع النصف إلى الآخر يصير مختاراً للفداء لأن الحق ثبت للمقتول، ولو صالح أحدهما على نصف العبد خير المولى والولي المدفوع إليه بين أن يدفعا نصف العبد إلى الثاني أو يفديا لأن الجناية انقلبت مالاً والعبد في ملكهما فيعتبر بمال لو جنى جناية خطأ والعبد ملكهما يخير بين الدفع والفداء فكذا هذا لأن العبد فرغ من نصف الجناية بالصلح وبقي مشغولاً بالنصف فثبت لهما الخيار في النصف. وإن صالح أحدهما عن جميع العبد قيل للشريك ادفع نصفه إلى أخيك أو أفده لأنه انتقل الملك إليه ونصفه مشغول بالجناية، ولو قتلت أمة رجلاً عمداً وله وليان فصالح المولى أحدهما على ولدها صار مختاراً للفداء في نصيب الآخر فيفديه بنصف الدية.
With one of them harm was not due upon him. And in killing by mistake, if there is one guardian and he chooses the ransom (fidaa) in half, it becomes a choice of ransom with respect to the other as long as the slave remains, because their right is established in the slave separately and jointly. And if the slave dies before he pays the half to the other, he becomes chooser of ransom because the right is established for the slain. And if one of them compromises upon half the slave, the master and the guardian to whom it is paid are given the choice between handing over half the slave to the second or paying ransom, because the offense has turned into property and the slave is in their joint ownership, so it is considered like property: if he had committed a mistaken offense and the slave was jointly owned, he would have the choice between handing over and ransom; likewise this, because the slave is freed from half the offense by compromise and remains occupied with half, so the choice is established for them in the half. And if one compromises for the entire slave, it is said to the partner: hand over his half to your brother or ransom it, because ownership has transferred to him and half of him is occupied with the offense. And if a female slave killed a man intentionally and he has two guardians, and the master compromised with one of them concerning her child, he becomes chooser of ransom for the share of the other, so he ransoms him with half the blood-money.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the master's khiyar (choice) when there are multiple heirs (awliyaa) to a victim killed by a slave. The passage discusses several scenarios: First, in a case of accidental killing (qatl al-khata'), if there is a single guardian (wali) who chooses fidaa (ransom) for half the slave, that counts as a choice of fidaa for the entire slave as long as the slave is still alive. This is because the rights of both heirs are established in the slave jointly but separately. If the slave dies before paying the half to the other, the guardian becomes bound to fidaa, because the right was for the slain person. Second, if one of the two guardians compromises (sulh) with the master for half the slave, then both the master and the guardian who received the half have a choice: they may either hand over the half to the second guardian or pay fidaa. This is because the jinaayah (offense) has transformed into a property right, and the slave is jointly owned by the master and the first guardian. They are treated like joint owners of property who have the choice between handing over the slave or paying fidaa, by analogy to a case where a jointly-owned slave commits a mistaken killing. Third, if one guardian compromises for the entire slave, the partner (the other guardian) is told to either hand over half of the slave to the other or ransom it, because the ownership of the whole slave has transferred to the compromising guardian, but half is still encumbered with the jinaayah. Fourth, if a female slave kills a man intentionally and there are two guardians, and the master compromises with one concerning her child (walad), that act makes the master bound to fidaa (ransom) for the share of the other guardian, which he must pay as half the diyah. The reasoning throughout is based on the principle that when an offense becomes a property obligation, the master's choice operates similarly to that of a joint owner.

Key Terms
ولي (waliy): In this context, the guardian or heir of the victim who has the right to demand qisas or diyah.
فداء (fidaa'): The ransom paid by the master to the victim's heir to keep the slave instead of handing him over.
دفع (daf'): The act of handing over the slave to the victim's heir in satisfaction of the jinaayah.
صلح (sulh): A compromise between the master and one of the heirs, often for part of the slave or her child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a slave's accidental killing with two guardians, if the master chooses fidaa for half the slave, what is the effect on the master's obligation?
A) He must pay half the diyah to each guardian.
B) He is deemed to have chosen fidaa for the entire slave while the slave is alive.
C) He may later choose to hand over the slave for the other half.
D) His choice becomes void if the slave dies.

Key Takeaway
When a master compromises with one of multiple heirs after a slave's jinaayah, the claim transforms into property, and the khiyar operates according to the rules of joint ownership.

Compromise with One Heir: Does It Bind the Master to Fidaa for the Other?
Presenting conflicting riwayaat on whether sulh with one heir makes master bound to fidaa for the rest

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi riwayaat on whether compromise with one heir constitutes choice of fidaa for the other, and identify which actions bind the master to fidaa.

Core Idea
Compromise for part of the slave's value may or may not imply choice for the whole, depending on the riwaya.

When a master's slave commits a crime (jinaayah), the victim's heirs have a right to either the slave (daf') or his value (fidaa). If the master compromises with one heir for part of the slave's value, does that mean he has chosen fidaa for the entire slave? The Hanafi school has two transmitted positions on this question. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning behind each riwaya and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two riwayaat and their rulings
The passage presents two riwayaat: one from al-Durar and another from al-Jami' and al-Durar. One holds that compromise with one heir does not constitute ikhtiyaar for fidaa; the other holds that it does. State each clearly.
2
Understand the reasoning of the first riwaya (compromise = ikhtiyaar)
The justification: ownership of the jinaayah right belongs to the deceased first, then passes to the heirs as a unit. Thus any choice regarding part of the slave's value is a choice for the whole, to prevent fragmentation.
This is presented as the justification of the riwaya from al-Jami'.
3
Understand the reasoning of the second riwaya (compromise ≠ ikhtiyaar)
The justification distinguishes between daf' and fidaa. Daf' (handing over the slave) may be compelled by necessity, so it does not imply intention. Fidaa (ransom) shows a desire to retain the slave. Compromise with part is ambiguous, so it does not amount to a choice for the whole.
This is the riwaya of sulh from al-Durar.
4
Identify actions that constitute ikhtiyaar
The passage lists actions that clearly indicate choice of fidaa: sale, gift, charity, emancipation, tadbir, kitabah, or inflicting a defect. Pawn (rahn), lease (ijara), and marriage (nikah) do not constitute ikhtiyaar in the apparent riwaya, though al-Tahawi differs on marriage.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to the first riwaya, the master becomes bound to fidaa for the entire slave; according to the second, he still has the option to hand over the remaining share to the other heir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر في كتاب الدرر لا مختاراً للفداء . يصير ولو صالح أحدهما في ثلث الأمة كان الثاني له خيار أن يدفعه أو يفديه. وفي الجامع والدرر : لا يكون منه اختياراً وجه هذه الرواية أنه سوى بين الدفع والفداء في البعض وذلك لأن الملك يقع للميت أولاً ثم ينتقل إلى الوارث لما بينا فكان ملك الميت أصلاً وملك الوارث بناء عليه فيكون المستحق للجناية واحداً، فاختيار الدفع والفداء في البعض يكون اختياراً في الكل لئلا يتفرق الملك على المستحق . الأمة تحصل من كلها لا من بعضها، وجه رواية الصلح وهو الفرق بين الدفع والفداء أن الإنسان قد يضطر إلى أن يخرج بعض العبد عن ملكه لكي يعيد الزائل إلى ملكه في الثاني، وإذا وجد ثمن فلا يكون اختيار دفع النصف اختيار دفع النصف الآخر دلالة، فأما اختيار بعض الفداء يدل على اختيار إمساك في . ملكه الرغبة لإمساكها المنافع تحصل له منها لا تحصل له من غيرها وتلك المنافع ا، فاختيار إمساك الأمة يدل على اختيار الفداء ضرورة اختيار الصلح أن يقول المولى اخترت الفداء أو الدلالة كما لو تصرف فيه بالبيع أو بالهبة أو بالصدقة أو بالعتق أو بالتدبير أو بالكتابة أو بعيب كفقء العين والجراحة وقطع اليد. وأما في الرهن والإجارة والنكاح كما لو تزوج منه امرأة وكانت أمة فتزوجها فهذا لا يكون اختياراً في ظاهر الرواية. وذكر الطحاوي أنه يصير مختاراً.
And it is mentioned in the book al-Durar: he does not become a chooser of ransom. And if one of them compromises for one-third of the female slave, the second has the option to hand over or ransom. And in al-Jami' and al-Durar: it does not constitute a choice. The justification of this riwaya is that it equalizes between handing over and ransom in part, because ownership first accrues to the deceased then transfers to the heir as we established, so the ownership of the deceased is original and the heir's ownership is derivative, thus the one entitled to compensation for the offense is one. So choice of handover or ransom in part is choice for the whole, lest ownership be divided among the entitled. The female slave is obtained [in full?] not in part. The justification of the riwaya of sulh is the distinction between handover and ransom: a person may be compelled to remove part of the slave from his ownership in order to return the lost to his ownership in the second [case?]; and if there is a price, then choosing to hand over half is not indicative of choosing to hand over the other half, whereas choosing part of the ransom indicates choosing to retain [the slave] because he desires to keep her for the benefits he obtains from her, which he does not obtain from others. So choosing to retain the female slave indicates choosing ransom necessarily. The choice of sulh is that the master says 'I choose fidaa' or indication, just as if he disposed of him by sale, gift, charity, emancipation, tadbir, kitabah, or by inflicting a defect such as gouging an eye, wounding, or cutting off a hand. As for pawn (rahn), lease (ijara), and marriage (nikah), such as if he marries a woman and she is a female slave and he marries her, this does not constitute a choice in the apparent riwaya. And al-Tahawi mentioned that he becomes a chooser.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether a compromise (sulh) with one heir for part of the slave makes the master bound to choose fidaa (ransom) for the remaining heir. Two riwayaat are presented: one from Kitab al-Durar and another from al-Jami' and al-Durar. According to the riwaya that it does not constitute choice, the reasoning is that ownership of the right to jinaayah belongs to the deceased (the victim) originally and then transfers to the heir. Therefore, the entitlement is one, so any choice regarding part of the slave's value counts as a choice for the entirety, to prevent fragmentation of ownership over the entitled party. The other riwaya (of sulh) distinguishes between handover (daf') and ransom (fidaa). Handing over part of the slave may be compelled for necessity, so it does not imply intention for the rest. But choosing to pay fidaa (ransom) for part shows intention to retain the slave, because the master desires the benefits of the slave. Thus choosing fidaa in part implies choosing fidaa entirely. The passage then lists various acts that constitute ikhtiyaar (choice) for fidaa by implication: selling the slave, giving him as gift, charity, emancipation, tadbir, kitabah, or even causing a defect deliberately. However, pawning (rahn), leasing (ijara), or marrying the female slave does not constitute a choice in the apparent riwaya. Al-Tahawi, however, holds that marrying her does constitute choice. This dispute is significant in determining whether the master becomes liable for full ransom or can still hand over the slave.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyaar): The master's act of choosing between fidaa and daf' for the slave's jinaayah, which if done explicitly or implicitly becomes binding.
رواية (riwaya): A transmission of a legal position, here referring to the different reports within the Hanafi school from Imam Muhammad's books.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the riwaya that does not consider compromise as ikhtiyaar, which of the following actions does NOT make the master bound to fidaa?
A) Selling the slave
B) Marrying the female slave
C) Gifting the slave
D) Emancipating the slave

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contains two riwayaat on whether compromise with one heir for part of the slave constitutes choice of fidaa; the majority holds it does, based on unity of ownership over the jinaayah right.

Rulings When a Slave Dies or Is Killed by Master or Third Party
Stating rulings when slave is no longer available due to death or killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for various scenarios of a slave's death or killing and determine when the master becomes liable for compensation (arsh) or must hand over the slave.

Core Idea
The master's liability for a slave's jinaaya changes drastically based on who kills the slave and with what intent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of rulings concerning a slave who is no longer available for handover due to death or killing. The rulings depend on whether the slave dies naturally, is killed by his master, by a third party, or by another slave. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly determining the master's obligations and the rights of victims' heirs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial scenario: slave dies before master chooses
If the slave dies naturally before the master makes a choice (between daf and fidaa), the offense becomes void and the master owes nothing.
2
Analyze scenario where master kills the slave
If the master kills the slave, he is deemed to have chosen to pay arsh (compensation) and becomes liable for it.
This is a key distinction: killing the slave is not an escape from liability.
3
Third party kills the slave: intentional vs mistaken
If a third party kills the slave intentionally, the jinaaya becomes void and the master may pursue qisas against the killer. If mistaken, the master takes the value (qimah) from the killer and must pay it to the offense's heirs.
The master cannot use the value as he wishes; it must go to the heirs.
4
Slave killed by another's slave
If a slave belonging to another kills the slave, the victim's heir has the choice to hand over the killer slave or demand ransom (fidaa). If handover occurs, the heir takes the killer slave.
The handed-over slave replaces the original in liability.
5
Slave killed by own master's other slave
If another slave of the same master kills the slave, the master has the choice between handing over the killer slave or paying fidaa equal to the value of the killed slave.
This differs from the third-party slave scenario.
6
Injuries by a third party slave
If a third-party slave injures the slave (cuts hand, gouges eye), the offender's master has the choice to hand over or pay arsh. The injured slave's master is then told to hand over his slave to the victim or pay ransom.
7
Emancipation and pardon scenarios
In mistaken killing, emancipation creates liability; in intentional killing, emancipation does not. If one son of the victim pardons, the other son's share becomes a property right; the slave must work to pay half his value, and the master is not liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أن العبد مات قبل أن يختار المولى شيئاً بطلت الجناية عمداً كانت أو خطأ ولا يؤخذ المولى بشيء، فإن لم يمت ولكن قتله مولاه فإنه يصير مختاراً للأرش، فإن لم يقتله مولاه ولكن قتله أجنبي، فإن كان عمداً بطلت الجناية وللمولى أن يقتص، وإن كان خطأ يأخذ القيمة ثم يدفع تلك القيمة إلى أولياء الجناية حتى لو تصرف في تلك القيمة لا يصير مختاراً للأرش. وكذلك لو قتله عبد فخير الولي بين الدفع والفداء ويدفع إلى ولي الجناية، ولو دفع العبد إلى مولى العبد المقتول قام مقامه لحماً ودماً كأنه هو فيخير المولى بالفداء حتى لو تصرف في العبد المدفوع بالبيع أو بالعتق أو نحوه فإن يصير مختاراً للفداء، ولو لم يقتله عبد الأجنبي ولكنه قتله عبد آخر لمولاه فإنه يخير المولى بين الدفع والفداء بقيمة العبد المقتول فإن دفعه العبد إليه سلم لهم، وإن اختار الفداء يفدى بقيمة العبد المقتول. ولو قطع الأجنبي يد هذا وفقأ عينه أو جراحة فيخير العبد الأجنبي، فإن دفع أو فداه بالأرش فإنه يقال لمولى العبد المفقوءة عينه ادفع عبدك هذا إلى ولي الجناية أو افده، وقيد الضمان في العتق يكون للقتل خطأ لأنه لو كان عمداً فأعتق لا يلزمه شيء، ولو كان العبد قتل رجلاً عمداً ووجب القصاص فأعتقه مولاه فلا يلزم المولى شيء، ولو كان للمقتول ولدان فعفا أحدهما بطل حقه وانقلب نصيب الآخر مالاً فله أن يستسعى العبد في نصف قيمته ولا يجب على المولى نصف القيمة.
And if the slave dies before the master chooses anything, the offense becomes void, whether intentional or mistaken, and nothing is taken from the master. And if he does not die but his master kills him, then he becomes a chooser of the arsh (compensation). And if his master does not kill him but a third party kills him, then if it was intentional, the offense becomes void and the master has the right to retaliation; and if it was mistaken, he takes the value and then pays that value to the heirs of the offense, even if he disposes of that value, he does not become a chooser of arsh. And likewise, if a slave kills him, the guardian has the choice between handing over and ransom, and he hands over to the guardian of the offense. And if the slave is handed over to the master of the killed slave, he stands in his place in flesh and blood as if he were him, so the master has the choice of ransom, even if he disposes of the handed-over slave by sale or emancipation or the like, then he becomes a chooser of ransom. And if a third-party slave does not kill him but another slave of his master kills him, then the master has the choice between handing over and ransom for the value of the killed slave; if he hands over the slave to them, they are delivered, and if he chooses ransom, he ransoms with the value of the killed slave. And if a third party cuts off this one's hand or gouges his eye or wounds, then the third-party slave has the choice; if he hands over or ransoms with the arsh, then it is said to the master of the slave whose eye was gouged: hand over this slave of yours to the guardian of the offense or ransom him. And the condition of guarantee in emancipation is for mistaken killing, because if it was intentional and he emancipates, nothing is incumbent upon him. And if the slave killed a man intentionally and retaliation becomes due, and his master emancipates him, nothing is incumbent upon the master. And if the victim has two sons and one of them pardons, his right becomes void and the share of the other turns into property, so he may have the slave work to earn half his value, and half the value is not incumbent upon the master.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings concerning situations where the slave is no longer available for handover due to death or being killed. The key scenarios are: (1) If the slave dies naturally before the master makes a choice, the jinaayah becomes void entirely, and the master owes nothing. (2) If the master himself kills the slave, he is deemed to have chosen to pay arsh (compensation), i.e., he becomes liable for fidaa. (3) If a third party kills the slave, the ruling differs based on intent: if intentional, the jinaayah becomes void and the master can pursue qisas against the killer; if mistaken, the master takes the value (qimah) from the killer and must pay it to the victim's heirs, but disposing of that value does not count as choosing arsh. (4) If a slave belonging to another kills the slave, the victim's heir has the choice between daf' (handover) and fidaa. If the handover occurs, the heir takes the killer slave. (5) If the killer slave is handed over to the master of the killed slave, that master becomes a chooser of fidaa for the original offense, and any disposition of the handed-over slave (sale, emancipation) constitutes ikhtiyaar for fidaa. (6) If a slave of the same master kills the slave, the master has the choice between handing over or paying fidaa for the value of the killed slave. (7) If a third party injures the slave (cuts hand, gouges eye), the slave (now the offender) has the choice; if he hands over or pays arsh, then the master of the injured slave is told to hand over his slave to the victim's heir or ransom him. (8) In emancipation, guarantee (daman) only applies in mistaken killing; in intentional killing, emancipation does not incur liability. (9) If a slave commits intentional murder and the master emancipates him after qisas becomes due, the master owes nothing. (10) If two sons of the victim and one pardons, that share is void and the other share becomes a property right; the slave must work to pay half his value; the master is not liable for that half. These rulings illustrate the complex interplay between the master's ownership, the slave's criminal liability, and third-party actions.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for physical injury or killing, similar to diyah but often for lesser injuries.
قيمة (qimah): The market value of the slave, paid as compensation when the slave is killed by a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave commits an offense and then the master kills him before making a choice, what is the ruling?
A) The offense becomes void and the master owes nothing.
B) The master becomes liable for arsh (compensation).
C) The master must hand over the slave's corpse.
D) The master may choose between daf' and fidaa posthumously.

Key Takeaway
The master's liability for a slave's jinaaya is determined by the manner of the slave's death, with killing by the master triggering compensation.

Combined Offenses: Property Destruction and Manslaughter by Slave
Ruling on combined claims against a slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the master's options when a slave commits both property destruction and accidental killing, and apply the rules regarding knowledge when selling the slave for debt.

Core Idea
The master's knowledge determines liability when selling a slave who owes both debt and jinaayah.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced scenario: a slave incurs a debt from destroying property and also commits accidental killing (jinaayah). Both creditors and the victim's heirs come to claim. The master has a choice to hand over the slave or pay ransom. However, if the slave is sold for debt, the master's knowledge of the jinaayah at the time of sale determines whether he becomes liable for ransom. The judge's knowledge also affects the validity of the jinaayah claim. This lesson unpacks these priority and liability rules, highlighting the interplay between financial and criminal claims in Hanafi slave law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
Note that the slave has two separate liabilities: property destruction (debt) and accidental killing (jinaayah on person). Creditors represent the financial claim; the victim's heirs represent the criminal claim.
The slave's neck is liable for the jinaayah.
2
Analyze the first scenario: creditors and heirs come together
The master is given a choice (khiyar) between daf' (handing over the slave to the heirs) and fida' (paying ransom). If he chooses daf', the slave is given to the heirs, but creditors can then pursue the slave in the hands of the heirs for their debt.
The master may also pay the debt to free the slave before sale.
3
Analyze the second scenario: creditors come first, master sells without judge
If the master sells the slave for the debt without a court order, his knowledge of the jinaayah affects the outcome: if he knew, he is deemed to have chosen ransom (fida'), making him liable for the slave's value (diyah). If he did not know, he owes the lesser of the slave's value or the debt.
Selling with knowledge constitutes ikhtiyaar for fida'.
4
Analyze when the judge sells for debt
If the judge sells the slave for debt without knowing about the jinaayah, the jinaayah is not nullified (the heirs can still claim). If the judge knows about the jinaayah, the sale voids the jinaayah claim (the heirs lose their right).
The judge's knowledge affects the validity of the criminal claim.
5
Synthesize the principles
The master's liability depends on his choice and his knowledge. The priority between creditors and heirs is flexible: if both come together, the master chooses; if creditors come first, the master's knowledge determines liability. The judge's role also impacts the outcome.
This shows the Hanafi concern for balancing different claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو كان العبد استهلك مالاً فوجب عليه وقتل آخر خطأ فحضر أصحاب الديون وأولياء الجناية معاً فإنه يخير المولى بين الدفع والفداء، فإن ظهرت رقبة العبد عن الجناية فبعد ذلك يباع في الدين إلا إذا قضى السيد الدين، وإن اختار الدفع دفعه إلى أولياء الجناية ثم يتبعونه في دينهم. وإن حضر أصحاب الديون أولاً فباع المولى العبد في دينهم بغير أمر القاضي فإنه ينظر؛ إن كان عالماً بالجناية صار مختاراً للفداء، وإن كان غير عالم بالجناية يلزمه الأقل من قيمته ومن الدين. وإن كان الدفع للقاضي فإن كان القاضي غير عالم بالجناية فباع العبد في الدين لم تبطل الجناية، وإن كان القاضي يعلم بالجناية فباعه في الدين بطلت الجناية.
And if the slave had destroyed property and it became due upon him, and he killed another by mistake, and the creditors and the heirs of the offense come together, then the master is given the choice between handover and ransom. If the slave's neck becomes free from the offense, then after that he is sold for the debt unless the master pays the debt. And if he chooses handover, he hands him over to the heirs of the offense, then they pursue him for their debt. And if the creditors come first and the master sells the slave for their debt without the order of the judge, then it is considered: if he was aware of the offense, he becomes a chooser of ransom; if he was unaware of the offense, he is liable for the lesser of his value and the debt. And if the handover is to the judge, then if the judge was unaware of the offense and sold the slave for the debt, the offense is not nullified; and if the judge was aware of the offense and sold him for the debt, the offense is nullified.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where a slave commits two offenses: destruction of property (financial liability) and accidental killing (jinaayah on the person). Both creditors (for the property) and heirs of the victim (for the killing) come to claim. The master has the initial choice between daf' (handing over the slave to the victim's heirs) or fidaa (paying ransom). If the master chooses handover, the slave is given to the heirs, but the creditors can then pursue the slave in the hands of the heirs for their debt. However, if the slave is first sold to satisfy the debt, the master's knowledge matters: if he knew of the jinaayah and sold the slave, that constitutes ikhtiyaar for fidaa (making him liable for ransom); if he did not know, he owes the lesser of the slave's value or the debt. When a judge sells the slave for debt, his knowledge of the jinaayah determines whether the jinaayah is voided: if the judge knows, the offense is void; if not, it remains. This shows the interplay between financial claims and criminal liability in slave law.

Key Terms
دين (dayn): Debt owed by the slave to creditors due to property destruction.
جناية (jinaayah): Criminal offense, here accidental killing.
دفع (daf'): Handing over the slave to the victim's heirs.
فداء (fida'): Ransom paid by the master to free the slave.
خيار (khiyar): Option/choice given to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slave commits both property destruction and accidental killing, and the creditors come first, and the master sells the slave for the debt without knowing about the killing, what is the master liable for?
A) Full diyah (slave's value)
B) The lesser of the slave's value or the debt
C) The debt only
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
The master's and judge's knowledge of the jinaayah is the decisive factor in determining liability and the priority of claims when a slave commits combined offenses.
